--- page 197 ---
From:
David Hunsaker
To:
Kathy Kleinbaum
Subject:
Fogbird - Per meeting on March 20th.
Date:
Monday, March 27, 2023 1:13:07 PM
Hi Kathy,
I was able to watch the meeting video this past weekend and wanted to pass along my
thoughts.  Thanks for all your time putting together the presentation you did.  Here are a few
of my comments that can be passed along to the board.
1. My concern is some of the board members mention about reopening our block between 1st
and 2nd.  I think is name was Hedges.  He said he walked the block to see how the foot traffic
was and he did it on a Sunday?  Why not Friday or Saturday night?  Sunday is the only day we
are closed due to how slow foot traffic is per the hours we would normally be open.

business on that street.  I know the emergency lane is a big concern about our pedestrian mall.

have the fire department feel safe with the lane.

welcoming.  Lighting just on the trees like we have is not enough.  How does San Carlos make
this happen and not us?
c. San Carlos has a farmers market on those closed blocks and they use the fire lane.  How
does San Carlos figure out how to have the fire department access, have parklets and have
farmers market stalls?  Appears they have sorted it out.
2. We have had very little city support for use of the two blocks.  I think we had 3 events in
Sept but then nothing.  We need to have more year round events on those blocks in order for
more activity to occur. 
3. Our block finally had two new locations open up this past month.  You can see one of these
lines due to popularity which is fantastic.  I would ask that you give our block more time to
see if other places open outdoor eating spaces.
4. I ask the board to approve the budget for both blocks.
I am planning on spending another $5,000 to update my space this spring but am concerned
once hearing the video that our block will be returned to street traffic.  
I look forward to hearing how the approval process goes for the pedestrian mall.

bikes, families walk their dogs, and the atmosphere just feels safer.
I have lived and worked in San Mateo since 2006, and am a lifelong Bay Area resident. I can firmly
recall the nightmare of trying to find a parking space along B street, the traffic jams that would back
up 3rd and 2nd Aves, the danger near the Caltrain at-grade crossings, and the noise. Looking at old
satellite photos, we only lost 32 parking spaces to the closure.

felt a bit underutilized lately, but I think that as the weather eases up and if we get a new mix of
businesses the street will once again become vibrant! Look at the absolute monster of a line that
Kajiken draws: a solid line of people waiting an hour before they open on a weekend. I recall when
Curry Up Now had their parklet set up and we used to dine there along with several other parties,
even up to last year. Even last fall, in front of Philz, I would hang around waiting for a sidewalk table
to open up in the mornings. Clearly, if there are outdoor spots, the people will come.
Please consider attracting more businesses that add to the vibrancy of the block. Promote events
and art. Bring back parklets and streetside tables. Let us not regress back to car-culture that 1) won't
add that much more parking 2) will degrade pedestrian safety and 3) will jam traffic along 2nd and

From: Ian Webster

Subject: Urging the City Council to Keep the 1st-2nd Ave B Street Pedestrian Mall
Dear Members of the San Mateo City Council,
I hope this letter finds you well. As a proud resident of San Mateo, I am writing to express my strong
support for maintaining the 1st-2nd Ave block on B Street as a pedestrian mall and to urge you not
to reopen it to cars. My wife and I have greatly enjoyed the walkable, welcoming atmosphere that
the street closure has brought to our downtown area, and we frequent locations on this block such
as Yifang, Koja Kitchen, and now Somi Somi and Kajiken.
The city has a great model for success: the 2nd Ave to 3rd Ave block!  The pedestrian mall on B
Street is cherished, attracting both residents and visitors alike. We are eager to see continued
investment in making it feel more inviting, lively, safe, and accessible.  It encourages people to spend
time outside, enjoy local businesses, and engage with one another.
Reopening B Street to cars would not only disrupt this thriving environment but would also have
negative consequences for the long-term development of our downtown. A pedestrian mall fosters a
sense of community, promotes local businesses, and reduces pollution and noise from vehicles.
These qualities support a sustainable, vibrant downtown that will continue to attract new businesses
and residents for years to come.
In conclusion, I respectfully request that the City Council prioritize the long-term benefits of a
pedestrian mall over the short-term convenience of reopening B Street to cars. By keeping B Street
closed to vehicles, we can continue to cultivate a thriving, walkable downtown that will be a source
of pride for all San Mateo residents.
Thank you for your time and consideration. I trust that you will make the best decision for our
community.

Subject: Urgent Request to make outdoor dining permanent to prevent restaurant closures 
  
  
 
Dear City Council Members, 
I hope this email finds you in good health and high spirits. I am writing to urgently request your support 
in making outdoor dining a permanent fixture in our city. The recent upliftment of COVID restrictions by 
the government is indeed a positive step forward. However, it is important to acknowledge that the fear 
and hesitancy among the public still persist, which significantly impacts the number of customers willing 
to dine inside restaurants. As a result, the livelihood of many local businesses, including our restaurant, 
is at stake. 
The pandemic has presented unprecedented challenges for the restaurant industry, with many 
establishments struggling to stay afloat. The introduction of outdoor dining has been a lifeline for us and 
countless others, allowing us to serve our customers while complying with safety protocols. However, if 
outdoor dining is discontinued, and with the looming recession, we face the grim possibility of having to 
close down our restaurant. 
Outdoor dining has not only provided a safe and comfortable dining experience for our customers but 
has also allowed us to expand our seating capacity and generate crucial revenue. It has been 
instrumental in mitigating the financial losses we have incurred during the pandemic and keeping our 
employees employed. By making outdoor dining permanent, you would be safeguarding the livelihoods 
of not just our restaurant staff but also numerous individuals and families who depend on the local 
hospitality industry for their income. 
The continuation of outdoor dining is essential not only for the survival of our business but also for the 
overall economic well-being of our city. The revenue generated by outdoor dining establishments 
contributes to the local economy, supporting suppliers, farmers, and other businesses in the hospitality 
sector. Additionally, outdoor dining has become an integral part of our city's identity, attracting visitors 
and enhancing the vibrancy of our community. 
I implore the City Council to consider the potential ramifications of discontinuing outdoor dining. The 
closure of restaurants, particularly those that have been pillars of our community for years, would not 
only lead to job losses but also result in the loss of cherished gathering places for residents. We must 
strive to strike a balance between public health and economic recovery, and making outdoor dining 
permanent is a crucial step towards achieving this equilibrium. 
Thank you for your attention and understanding of the challenges we face as a local business. I kindly 
request the City Council to prioritize the long-term sustainability of our restaurant and others like ours 
by making outdoor dining a permanent feature in our city. Your support will not only safeguard our 
business but also contribute to the revival and prosperity of our community as a whole. 
I appreciate your time and consideration. Should you require any further information or support on this 
matter, please do not hesitate to reach out.

From: 
Arielle Angel < 
Sent on: Monday, May 15, 2023 12:31:54 AM 
To:

CC: 
Clerk <clerk@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: Comment on Agenda Item 14 (Pedestrian Mall – Boundary Determination) for 
General Council Meeting on 5/15 
  
  
 
Dear Councilmembers  
 
My name is Arielle Angel and I am a San Mateo resident. I highly support keeping B 
St. between 1st and 2nd closed to vehicles all year round, and I have a number of 
reasons why:  
 
1. I've been in San Mateo throughout the pandemic and have seen the benefits of 
outdoor dining when restaurants had patios out front between 1st and 2nd, when 
permitting for outdoor spaces was easier. I've continued to see heavy pedestrian 
activity between 1st and 2nd especially with the addition of Kajiken and Somi Somi ( 
in fact, any given night that Somi Somi is open late, you'll see lots of people using 
Urban Ritual's benches to enjoy their ice cream, proving that if there were more 
seating options, people will enjoy them). It's hard for me to believe that leaning in on 
pedestrianized street with a plethora of seating options would reduce foot traffic and 
hurt businesses...I think the opposite is true. In fact, I've heard several people say they 
visit Wursthall less ever since they got rid of their outdoor beer garden, which is such 
a shame.  
 
2. I think we're incorrectly putting the onus on businesses to carry the burden of 
making the street more lively with building outdoor patios when it really should be the 
city driving and executing on the idea of pedestrianized streets. I've been to several 
similarly sized cities and neighborhoods in California and around the country where 
something as simple as benches, fun street painting, wine barrels, or planters were 
utilized to attract people to buy food and enjoy on the pedestrian street. These streets 
were packed, lively, and kept businesses busy throughout open hours. These small 
adjustments don't get in the way of delivery or emergency vehicles, and in fact made 
it easier for emergency vehicles to access businesses as parked cars were not in the 
way.  
 
3. It seems a bit odd that a decision is being made based on just 20 responses. I feel 
like this issue concerns the hundreds of citizens who enjoy and utilize the pedestrian 
street every day and we're ignoring their voices. In fact, based on a resident survey of 
211 of 1058

--- page 212 ---
738 participants, folks voted largely in favor of keeping the street closed to cars (82% 
in fact).  
 
4. Finally at the end of the day, once again, we are entertaining the idea of making it 
easier for those who own a vehicle to enjoy B st., making it less safe and accessible 
for those without a car. This is not equitable. Owning a vehicle in today's economic 
climate is a heavy financial burden, so many people choose to live without one. We 
should be taking this into consideration every single time the city is considering any 
infrastructure improvement.  
 
Thank you for your time.

Dear Council, 
 
Regarding agenda item 14 tonight, I urge you to preserve B st as a pedestrian mall including between 1st 
and 2nd street, and to allow businesses to build parklet platforms. 
 
It takes political courage to override the wishes of a majority of the businesses on that block. And the 
health of our small business community is critical. But our downtown streets belong to the people of the 
city, not just the current businesses on the bloc. We need to look towards building the San Mateo of the 
future, not of the past. 
 
A future where mass transit, biking, and walking make up a significantly greater proportion of travel than it 
does today. 
 
A future where downtown residential density will increase to meet housing goals, giving us a greater 
population of people nearby to take advantage of these streets. 
 
And a future where the city beautifies these streets and helps it fill out with parklets, leading businesses to 
feel supported and see the positive impact of a beautiful, people-first city core. 
 
When I moved here in March of 2022, B street was a thriving communal place. All that changed when the 
city forced businesses to tear down their parklets and we entered an exceptionally long rainy season. The 
city has not done right by these local businesses. Instead of using B street for festivals, we should 
support great parklet structures and even consider allocating funding towards this effort to support these 
businesses. And these blocks should be beautified, not left as barren open pavement. 
 
When I visit the downtown of cities on the peninsula, such as Redwood City, I am embarrassed for San 
Mateo. Peninsula cities have proven that beautiful, functional pedestrian malls can enhance quality of life 
and support businesses. San Mateo can and must do better than we have here, and reducing our 
pedestrian space would be a step even further backwards. 
 
Respectfully, 
- Richard Ballard 
San Mateo Resident

From: 
Curtis  
Sent on: Saturday, May 13, 2023 9:19:33 PM 
To: 
Clerk <clerk@cityofsanmateo.org>; City Council (San 
Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: Support B. Street Closure to Cars! 
  
  
 
Hello, 
I'm reaching out as a resident of San Mateo County and a frequenter of downtown San 
Mateo. 
 
Over the past few years, I've watched downtown San Mateo change in a wonderful way. 
For the first time ever, I've seen cyclists, pedestrians, runners, and everyone in between 
share a communal space for dining, entertainment, and togetherness. Without the 
closure of streets to vehicle traffic, this would simply be impossible.  
 
I've always been in favor of designing spaces catered toward humans. When walking on 
the sidewalk of a street with traffic, I feel separated from my fellow humans by a river of 
pollution, noise, and fear. Imagine two embankments of a river, both with stores, food, 
and liveliness, but disconnected from one another. 
 
Please, when making this decision Monday night, remember the human. Remember the 
family who sits in the street and enjoys the evening sunset. Remember the cyclist who 
takes this route to avoid risking his own life. Remember the human. 
 
Thank you for your time and consideration. 
 
Sincerely, 
Curtis Draheim

227 of 1058

--- page 228 ---
From: 
L. Eberhart  
Sent on: Saturday, May 13, 2023 6:42:34 PM 
To: 
Clerk <clerk@cityofsanmateo.org>; City Council (San 
Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: Pedestrian B Street Mall 
  
  
 
May13, 2023 
  
Honorable Mayor Amourence Lee 
Deputy Mayor Lisa Diaz Nash 
Council Members Adam Loraine, Robert Newsom, and Rich Hedges 
clerk@cityofsanmateo.org 
citycouncil@cityofsanmateo.org 
  
Dear Mayor and City Council, 
 
I'm writing in response to an article I read in the San Mateo Daily Journal on the reopening of one block of 
B Street, specifically the one between 1st and 2nd street and the proposed discussion of this issue on 
May 15th.  I am very concerned that some members of council are considering only doing one block of B 
street due to some limited research into the proposed pedestrian mall proposal.    The issues cited in the 
article are: lack of use and atmosphere; lack of parking or difficulty parking for patrons and/or delivery 
food drivers; and lack of accessible parking stalls.  The article also states that a staff report was 
conducted showing dissatisfaction from the businesses on B street at the lack of pedestrian use and 
parking difficulties. 
 
First, I am very curious as to when the staff report was conducted.  If it occurred anytime AFTER the 
parklets were deconstructed (approx. May of 2022) then the study is tainted.  The atmosphere and 
pedestrian use of B Street mall has suffered and changed since the parklet removal mandate which 
required all businesses to remove their parklets or pay for the monthly use of the parking spaces.  Prior to 
the parklet removal B Street was, if not thriving, then at least becoming an activated outdoor space like 
Burlingame and San Carlos's outdoor dining areas.  San Mateo's City mandate to remove or pay even 
applied to the businesses that had constructed some very expensive level eating decks with shade 
structures in the 2-block proposed Pedestrian Mall.  Including these businesses, while fair to the other 
downtown businesses, in the parklet removal right before the summer season caused a definite lessening 
of pedestrian activation.  BlueBottle had a wonderful chic wood deck that it was forced to dismantle and 
Dahlia had a very active outdoor dining area that was probably not helped by its removal.  Some small 
enterprising businesses have managed to put out some non-permanent rolling landscaping and moveable 
seating.  But the danger of coffee or lunch rolling off a table that is sitting on sloped asphalt is real.  Also, 
sitting outside now without permanent shade structures has become uncomfortable, hot and not very 
nice.  The Phase 2 planned widening of the sidewalk for level eating areas will greatly help the 
atmosphere and comfort of the pedestrian mall, but currently the businesses are not able to envision this 
or are willing to wait for this to happen.  Please let these businesses create more permanent parklets 
again until the leveling of the street is able to be realized.  It will make the pedestrians more likely to 
return. 
 
The second issue with businesses believing that a few parking spaces adjacent to their business is going 
to magically solve their patrons desire for a short walk from car to business is not logical.  San Mateo has 
plenty of parking in parking garages, but very few people are willing to walk the extra 1/4 mile to utilize 
them.  Closing B Street between 2nd and 1st is actually the one block that has the most parking available 
228 of 1058

--- page 229 ---
with the adjacent parking garage.  If food delivery (UBER etc) parking is desired, provide for short timed 
parking within the ground floor of the parking garage.  Businesses that have opened since the parklet 
removal do not have any idea of what B street used to look like with pedestrians spending time eating 
outdoors in front of businesses.  Relating a lack of customers to parking is not a realistic 
equation.  Pedestrian malls have been shown to actually generate more business for retailers.  I think it 
would be a great idea if planning staff and the council looked into actual examples of successful 
pedestrian street transitions before scrapping what could actually be a boon to the businesses on B 
street. 
 
Finally, there are currently 8 accessible spaces within the ground floor of the parking garage adjacent to 
Main street.  To my knowledge the B street pedestrian mall did not remove ANY accessible street parking 
spots, therefore there has been no removal of accessible spaces due to the pedestrian mall.  If I'm wrong, 
it appears that there are plans to add accessible parking on 2nd street and this argument seems to be a 
mute point. 
 
I have been saddened by the lessening of the B-street pedestrian mall's vibrancy since the removal of the 
parklets, but I believe if the Council would continue to research and offer concessions such as level 
parklets and semi-permanent shade areas until Phase 2 occurs B street could once again become 
revitalized.  I realize that this would be a cost to the businesses, but perhaps this is something that the 
downtown business association could come up with a creative fundraiser for?  I would even like to 
propose that the B-street pedestrian mall continue down to the train station as I believe the activated area 
outside Wursthall during the pandemic was highly used.  Continuing the pedestrian mall would enhance 
the connection between public transit and the core of downtown 3rd street. 
 
Thank you for your consideration of these issues.  I have great respect for all the moving parts and 
careful attention to the needs of those involved that is required.  I look forward to seeing what the City 
Council can create for downtown. 
 
Sincerely, 
Leane Eberhart 
San Mateo Resident

Subject: Save the Pedestrian Mall! 
  
  
 
San Mateo City Council Members; 
 
I infer from the "parklet" platform proposal that City staff is very aware of potential 
advantages to strategically removing on-street motor vehicle parking.   
 
As a recent transplant from Santa Cruz, where I lived with several versions of the 
Pacific Avenue "Garden Mall" I urge City Council to take this opportunity to 
maximize pedestrian traffic on B Street in a long-term way.  
 
Business owners are typically experts in their specific businesses, but it is important 
that they not be mistaken for City planners.  
 
Anecdotal reports of customer dissatisfaction could be examined by an objective 
person if the date and context of the complaints were known, but very few of us live 
in a laboratory.  
 
After documenting more than 2000 hours of deliveries I made in downtown Santa 
Cruz, I saw very few instances where cars in the downtown area weren't more of an 
obstacle than a mobility tool. 
 
Thanks for your careful consideration of this important question.  
 
 
 
Jesse Frey  
 
Regular visitor from San Carlos.

From: 
Matt Harray  
Sent on: Sunday, May 14, 2023 3:52:41 PM 
To: 
City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: Save the Pedestrian Mall! 
  
  
 
To the City Council, 
 
My wife and I moved to San Mateo in early 2022 and were delighted by the 
impressive ecosystem of outdoor dining downtown. We spent a weekend here before 
moving to get a feel for the town, and ate at a great outdoor restaurant on the B Street 
pedestrian mall. Imagine my dismay as I was biking to work one day, after we moved 
here, and saw restaurant after restaurant dismantling nearly all of the outdoor dining 
spaces--due to onerous city requirements and fees. 
 
Simply visit neighboring San Carlos or Burlingame (as we now do when we want to 
eat out) to see how successful and charming outdoor dining can be! 
 
I was thrilled to learn that those 2 blocks of B Street would become permanent 
pedestrian spaces...but now that plan is also under threat. I'm perplexed and 
disheartened. I would love to see most of the downtown core turned to pedestrian 
malls, a strategy that has enlivened many city centers in Europe. 
 
I'm baffled that the city isn't taking the win-win of easy pedestrian/bicycle access: 
downtown would be far more inviting, drawing in more money to benefit local 
businesses. In the meantime, when we want to eat out on a lovely spring day, we will 
most likely head to San Carlos.  
 
Downtown San Mateo has plenty of parking, including multiple parking garages. For 
the sake of the climate, the economy, and the local quality of life, please ensure that--
at the very least--two blocks of B Street remain closed to cars. 
 
Sincerely, 
 
Matt Harray 
Hillsdale neighborhood

From: 
Daniel Hoekwater  
Sent on: Saturday, May 13, 2023 8:51:14 PM 
To: 
City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: Keep B Street Car-Free; Save the Pedestrian Mall! 
  
  
 
Dear San Mateo City Council,  
I am writing to you today to express my strong support for keeping B Street car-free. I 
am a San Mateo resident who moved here within the past year, and the pedestrian mall 
was a big part of what drew me to the city. I love the way the pedestrian mall makes 
downtown feel like a lively, accessible gathering space. It is a great place to walk, bike, 
and socialize with friends and family. I also feel very safe in the pedestrian mall, 
knowing that there are no cars to worry about.  
I understand that there have been some concerns raised by some businesses about the 
impact of the pedestrian mall on their bottom line. However, I believe that these 
concerns are unfounded. In fact, a recent resident survey showed that we 
overwhelmingly want a pedestrian mall. Of 738 respondents, 82% wanted B Street 
between 1st and 2nd to remain closed; 87% wanted B Street between 2nd and 3rd to 
remain closed (source).  
In addition, walkable, people-friendly streets are more economically productive. 
Communities with more walkable streets have higher property values, more jobs, and 
more tax revenue. (source)  
The businesses between 2nd and 3rd invested in outdoor areas and created a vibrant 
atmosphere. Even the businesses between 1st and 2nd that ask for the street to reopen 
to cars acknowledge the success of the businesses on the other block. We should be 
trying to replicate that success, not reopen to cars.  
The City hasn’t done enough to make this space inviting and attractive. We should be 
helping those businesses to thrive, especially going into summer:  
• 
Reduce the onerous requirements for outdoor dining areas and parklet 
construction. 
• 
Provide financial support to build inviting outdoor spaces (funding source - 
market rate parking fees). 
• 
Build an inviting space with lighting, greenery and seating. 
245 of 1058

--- page 246 ---
We shouldn’t make long-lasting policy based on the desires of less than 20 businesses 
with a high turnover rate. We should base long-term policy on the people who live, work, 
and recreate in San Mateo.  
I urge you to keep B Street car-free. It is a vital part of our downtown and it makes our 
city a better place to live, work, and play.  
Sincerely,  
Daniel Hoekwater

From: 
musu jangga  
Sent on: Sunday, May 7, 2023 9:51:50 PM 
To: 
City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: Comments regarding the Pedestrian Mall 
  
  
 
 
Dear City Council Members,  
This is Weiying from Koja Kitchen. I am writing to express my concern regarding the pedestrian 
mall located on 1st and 2nd Ave.. As a merchant of this city for the last 3 years, I have noticed a 
significant decrease in foot traffic in the area since the implementation of the pedestrian mall.  
It is my belief that the street should be reopened to vehicle traffic in order to increase foot 
passage and stimulate economic growth in the area. Many of the businesses located on the block 
of 1st and 2nd Ave. have reported a decrease in sales since the pedestrian mall was put in place, 
and I believe that reopening the street would help to alleviate this issue.  
Furthermore, I have observed that many pedestrians are avoiding the area altogether due to the 
lack of foot traffic and the inconvenience of having to navigate around the barriers and restricted 
areas. This has resulted in a negative impact on the overall vibrancy of the downtown area and 
has left many businesses struggling to stay afloat.  
In light of these observations, I would like to request that the City Council consider reopening 
the street at an earlier date. I believe that this would help to stimulate economic growth in the 
area.  
Thank you for your time and attention to this matter.  
Sincerely,

From:

Subject: Public Comment on Pedestrian Mall 
  
  
 
Hello, 
 
My name is Jill; I'm a professor, writer, and resident of San Mateo since 2011. I'd like 
to voice my strong support for keeping the pedestrian mall open, and to allow 
construction of new parklets.  
 
I understand the need for parking, but I think, with the new parking garages (which 
are never full!) and new 24-minute parking spaces for picking up orders/deliveries), 
the benefits FAR outweigh the need for parking. Yes, many people are working from 
home and using delivery apps. Yes, we need deliveries/trash collection. But many 
people working from home would love to take a walk to get coffee. To take a meeting. 
To grab lunch and get exercise. But right now, it's a "dead zone," as survey takers 
noted, because we have put nothing there. Simply closing the street is not enough.  
 
I love downtown San Mateo, and I've loved it even more since the birth of my 
daughter and the closure of streets to traffic. It's far more pleasant. Every resident I 
know feels this way. The walkable mall encourages me to visit more local businesses-
-I visit much more often now that we can safely walk. But I noticed on recent visits 
that there is hardly anywhere to sit! There are many people walking around who have 
clearly visited multiple businesses (bubble tea in one hand, takeout food in another, 
etc), but they cannot sit and enjoy (or decide on impulse to visit another business) 
easily without more parklets. The current design is not inviting people to stay, to 
spend more money; instead, it's encouraging them to leave.  
 
Adding more places for pedestrians to sit and enjoy what they purchase will allow for 
an even more vibrant downtown, and encourage more engagement with our local 
businesses. When you make the mall a more pleasant place to be, I am certain that 
people will use it even more than they already are. Every parent I know loves that 
there are places that are closed to the noise and the danger of cars. Many of us miss 
the outdoor dining that popped up during the pandemic and is now gone. But outdoor 
dining is much more pleasant without the exhaust smells and noise of traffic. We have 
such wonderful weather most of the year--let's take advantage of it! If we want our 
downtown to stay vibrant, we have to invest in making it welcoming. Adding more 
traffic with more cars will never do that. Walkable cities are better cities. 
260 of 1058

--- page 261 ---
When I visited Europe many years ago, that walkability was something I couldn't 
ignore. In every city, people spent time outside nearly all day. San Mateo is already a 
destination--I often hear (and have read) that people visit San Mateo when other cities 
don't feel as accessible. Let's make San Mateo a city that we can be even more proud 
of. Please be bold, and invest in making the pedestrian mall beautiful--at least 
seasonally, if not permanently. Please think long-term instead of short-term. I love our 
downtown businesses, and I want them to stay open. I really believe this is the way to 
do so. 
 
Thank you! 
Jill

Jill Kolongowski

From:

Sent on: Monday, May 15, 2023 12:54:53 AM

City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: We want B street to stay car free, and we want more outdoor dining! 
  
  
 
Dear City Councilmembers, 
 
During the general meeting scheduled for 5/15, you will be asked to decide if San 
Mateo will keep B Street between 1st and 2nd closed to automobiles.  I urge you to 
keep B Street car free! 
 
At issue during your decision making process is the results of a survey of 20 business 
owners on B Street that will be presented on 5/15.  I ask you, why is the survey of a 
small sample being given such large consideration? From an earlier survey of 
the entire city, 738 respondents, 82% wanted B Street between 1st and 2nd to remain 
closed; 87% wanted B Street between 2nd and 3rd to remain closed (source). 
 
Additionally, I ask Council and City staff, why wasn't a survey given out to all 
downtown San Mateo business owners about restaurant business success due to the 
parklet program?  I doubt that the additional revenue that businesses like Gao, 
Wursthall, and Taishoken received due to amazing outdoor dining options from the 
pilot parklet program have been taken into consideration.  Using only one survey of 
20 business owners (80% of new businesses will fail in 2 years) over surveys of city 
residents and the entire business community is an inadequate tool for determining to 
keep B Street car free. 
 
I'm curious as to why no research was given to understanding the lack of new parklets 
on the 1st and 2nd block of B Street. Philz, Curry Up Now, and Dalia all had parklet 
structures that the city forced them to destroy. Lack of support and direction meant 
1st/2nd street businesses refused to build new parklets, keeping B Street a barren 
space.  The city has had an opportunity for 3 years to implement a loan or revenue 
sharing program to encourage businesses to build parklets not only on B Street, but 
around the city.  Instead, due to lack of engagement and investment from the city, 
parklet spaces around San Mateo remain at incredible lows compared to March 2021 
when the pilot program was in effect. 
 
Due to the open space, I also ask, why has the city refused to greenlight any quick 
builds of public amenities on B Street? The city does nothing to make the space an 
inviting place to commune and solicit feedback on quick build designs, and then has 
no real answer when businesses complain that the 1st/2nd block of B Street is dead. 
262 of 1058

--- page 263 ---
Why has the city not followed through with using the block as an event space?  One 
or two festivals a year is a massive underutilization of the space.  A weekly farmer's 
market could easily bring in pedestrian traffic that has beneficial spillover effects for 
neighboring businesses. 
 
Releasing cars onto B Street will turn it back into a parking lot, the likes of which we 
see at Costco or any other car centric suburbia.  If City Council wants to reopen B 
Street to traffic causing cars, then I urge Councilmembers to run a circulation study 
of downtown San Mateo first, with the specific direction of understanding how 
pedestrian infrastructure allows for far more people to travel through dense city 
streets, unlike auto centric infrastructure that easily clogs up our downtown streets.  A 
freeway, downtown San Mateo is certainly not! 
 
Thank You, 
Sean Lacson

From: 
Tyson Mao  
Sent on: Monday, May 15, 2023 6:58:58 PM 
To: 
City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org> 
CC: 
Clerk <clerk@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: B St. Pedestrian Mall 
  
  
 
Hello City Council, 
 
Attached are my comments on Agenda Item #14 for today's Regular 
Meeting, Pedestrian Mall - Boundary Determination: 
 
Dear San Mateo City Council, my name is Tyson Mao and I am the owner 
of Wursthall. We are a restaurant and biergarden located at Baldwin 
Ave and B St. 
 
When San Mateo closed streets and made the space available for outdoor 
dining, we participated by placing outdoor tables and benches for the 
community. I, like many others, found it to be an extremely pleasant 
experience. It was a literal breath of fresh air, and I feel that many 
would agree that the relaxed atmosphere of locals enjoying some food, 
drink, and socialization was a welcome contribution of vibrancy to San 
Mateo. 
 
While covid was difficult, outdoor dining allowed Wursthall to reach 
our pre-pandemic revenue numbers. We were able to retain our staff and 
provide assistance through community donations. Wursthall was running 
at an annual revenue rate of approximately $3.2M when we had outdoor 
dining. 
 
When San Mateo removed the outdoor dining space, we immediately saw a 
sharp decline in traffic. Wursthall’s annual revenue rate dropped more 
than $1M down to $2M which meant we had to lay off staff and reduce 
hours. I know that 44%, or 8 of the businesses between 1st and 2nd, 
said that closing the street led to reduced revenues, but for us it 
was the exact opposite. Reopening our street - replacing our 
biergarden with parking and traffic - led to a 33% drop in revenue. 
 
This is also consistent with the fact that it is unlikely that a 
parking space outside of my business is occupied by a customer that is 
specifically visiting my business. There are many businesses in 
downtown San Mateo, and statistically, it is not realistic that an 
immediate parking space results in a meaningful amount of business 
revenue. 
 
As of today, Wursthall is no longer profitable without the outdoor 
space. While our business will do the best that we can to cut 
additional costs and improve other business metrics, I will be forced 
to close the business in September of 2025 after our lease term if we 
cannot turn the business around. 
 
San Mateo has offered a program to install parklets on parking spaces, 
but it is not financially viable. With the increase of construction 
270 of 1058

--- page 271 ---
materials and labor, an outdoor parklet would cost our business $70k 
and given that the area only covers two parking spaces, I cannot seat 
a sufficient number of customers to pay back the construction expense. 
It is much easier to monetize the pedestrian mall as we can create 
outdoor seating in an economically sensible way. 
 
The city and local business should collaborate to help increase the 
vibrancy of the block between 1st and 2nd on B St. Our business served 
as an example that there is an appetite by the community for outdoor 
gathering. The city can invest in cheap outdoor seating like picnic 
tables, and can allow other businesses to use the blocks: food trucks 
and street carts as an example. Wursthall is happy to support these 
efforts as much as possible, and we’re happy to explore cross 
promotion, events, and other mechanisms to help activate the block. 
 
To summarize, I would like to speak in support of keeping the 
pedestrian mall between 1st and 2nd available for businesses and I am 
happy to share our learnings and to assist in creating spaces that 
help foster a closer sense of community. 
 
-Tyson

Hello City Council, 
 I am writing to you in support of keeping B St between 1st and 2nd Avenues  closed  to vehicular 
 traﬃc. 
 In March 2021, during the height of the pandemic, the City of San Mateo conducted a  community 
 survey about the B St closure . Of the 738 respondents, 87% (580) wanted B St between 2nd and 3rd 
 Avenues to remain closed and 82% (544) wanted B St between 1st and 2nd Avenues to remain 
 closed: 
 70% of respondents (494) wanted the street closure to be year-round: 
 57% of respondents (421) said they would visit downtown more often if B St was closed to vehicular 
 traﬃc.  Only 7% (54) said they would visit less often : 
272 of 1058

--- page 273 ---
When the City decided to keep B St closed between 1st and 3rd Avenues, it required the businesses 
 along the pedestrian mall to remove their permanent parklets. What happened next was a literal A/B 
 Test on the eﬀects of outdoor infrastructure. 
 The businesses between 2nd and 3rd decided to “rebuild”, investing in ﬂexible outdoor seating, 
 shade, and partitions that could be dismantled and removed for street festivals. The result has been 
 a vibrant outdoor atmosphere that is bustling on evenings and weekends. The success of this eﬀort 
 is reﬂected in the business owners’ survey results. 
 67% of the business owners between 2nd and 3rd (10 of 15) believe that the street closure has 
 increased foot traﬃc and 40% (6 of 15) believe it has increased revenues: 
273 of 1058

--- page 274 ---
63% of the business owners between 2nd and 3rd (10 of 16) would like their block to remain closed 
 to vehicular traﬃc year-round: 
 The businesses between 1st and 2nd, on the other hand, primarily chose not to reinvest. Only 
 Fogbird and Urban Ritual have provided outdoor seating. The result is a block that feels inactive and 
 underutilized. 
 Unsurprisingly, 39% of the business owners between 1st and 2nd (7 of 18) believe that the street 
 closure has reduced foot traﬃc and 44% (8 of 18) believe it has decreased revenues: 
274 of 1058

--- page 275 ---
63% of the business owners between 1st and 2nd (12 of 19) would like their block to reopen to 
 vehicular traﬃc year-round: 
 I believe the lack of vibrancy between 1st and 2nd is due to lack of outdoor infrastructure , not 
 lack of interest or demand from patrons. Even without infrastructure the block attracts a fair 
 amount of activity during evenings and weekends. I took these photos earlier today (Sunday, May 
275 of 1058

--- page 276 ---
14th 2023). As is typical during peak hours, there was a long line of patrons waiting to get into 
 Kajiken. 
 It was 71 degrees outside and quite hot in the direct sunlight so patrons kept trying to wait against 
 the building in the shade. During the 30 minutes I waited in line, staﬀ came out a handful of times to 
 ask patrons to please wait in the street so they weren’t blocking the other entrances. Some

I believe that over time these storefronts will transition to businesses that are interested in 
 monetizing the pedestrian mall and investing in a vibrant atmosphere. In the meantime I believe the 
 City Council should consider easing that transition by the following: 
 1.  Run an experiment over the summer 
 The weather is just starting to get nice. Perhaps Parks and Rec has some extra picnic tables 
 that could be placed on the pedestrian mall between 1st and 2nd? Or maybe the City could 
 use some of the general funds to rent outdoor furniture for the summer? I think that “if you 
 build it, they will come”. If I am correct, and the vibrancy of this block is increased with 
 outdoor furniture, perhaps that will encourage the current businesses to invest in outdoor 
 infrastructure and monetize the space. 
 2.  Allow other businesses to activate the block 
 If the businesses between 1st and 2nd are unwilling or unable to activate the pedestrian 
 mall, allow other businesses to monetize the block. Some ideas: 
 ● 
 Allow the businesses on the adjacent blocks (between Baldwin and 1st and between 
 2nd and 3rd) to provide outdoor dining between 1st and 2nd 
 ● 
 Allow food trucks and food carts between 1st and 2nd 
 ● 
 Host a farmer’s market between 1st and 2nd every weekend 
 3.  Facilitate the transition to businesses that want to activate the block 
 It’s my understanding that all of the restaurants between Baldwin and 1st wanted their block 
 to remain closed to vehicular traﬃc, with the exception of China Bee which has since shut 
 down. Some of these businesses saw substantial revenue losses after their block reopened. 
 It’s extremely unfortunate that all of the restaurants on one block want their block to be 
 closed, but it’s currently open, while 6 of 11 restaurants on the adjacent block want their 
 block to be open, but it’s currently closed. Perhaps there is a creative solution - could the city 
 help facilitate lease swaps between these businesses? 
 Longer term, perhaps the city could help attract businesses that want to monetize the 
 pedestrian mall by providing incentives (funding? reduced taxes?) for businesses that invest 
 in outdoor infrastructure? The city could also consider requiring new businesses to provide 
 outdoor seating. 
 Ultimately, I don’t think the City should make long-term policy decisions based on the desires of a 
 small number of businesses (12) that historically have a high rate of turnover. (Will this decision be 
 revisited every time there’s substantial turnover?) Rather,  I think the City should prioritize the 
 opinions of the people who live, work and patronize San Mateo  when making policy decisions, 
 as reﬂected in the community survey of 738 people.

 pedestrian mall between 1st and 2nd Avenue and help facilitate the transition to businesses that 
 want to activate the space.

Subject: On B Street 
  
  
 
Dear Councilmembers, 
 
I encourage the council to re-frame the discussion this evening about B Street 
pedestrianization to survey the community's needs and wants--not the existing 
merchants. 
 
To re-frame it effectively: 
 
There are possibly more residential tenants in 2nd floor apartments on B Street 
between 1st & 2nd than there are merchants--they were of course not surveyed 🙂🙂 
 
B Street is an investment decision about investing in ourselves, not an investment in 
an existing group of merchants--a group of merchants who have demonstrated an 
unwillingness to co-invest in the public space so far. 
 
It's important to remember that merchants come and go at shockingly high turnover 
(~50% fail in 5 years). 
 
This statistic is not unique to downtown San Mateo. 
 
But investment into a permanent infrastructure for B Street will be for decades to 
come--while sizeable capital investment it will have very low recurring operational 
costs. 
 
Most importantly this decision about B Street sets the tone for the city's future: 
whether San Mateo is a destination for humans or for cars? 
 
If it is too difficult to get a read on the success or failure of pedestrianization right 
now council can postpone the decisionmaking--and expect to see the flourishing foot 
traffic the summer season will bring to the street. 
 
Yours truly, 
Max Mautner

--- page 286 ---
From: 
Jon New  
Sent on: Sunday, May 14, 2023 11:03:36 PM 
To: 
City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org> 
CC: 
Clerk <clerk@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: Please keep B St. closed to cars. 
  
  
 
Hi, I live near downtown San Mateo, and I'm strongly in favor of keeping B St. pedestrianized 
and closed to cars. 
 
Plenty of research has shown that pedestrian-oriented streets are more economically productive 
than any other kind of development. Strong Towns has demonstrated this as has Resilience, a 
non-profit dedicated to transitioning away from fossil fuels.  
 
This makes economic sense. Restaurants are essentially in the business of renting out real 
estate per hour, and one way to increase revenue is to increase real estate. In San Mateo, 
restaurants like Pausa and Avenida have been able to cut through our onerous parklet 
restrictions, and they’ve been rewarded with parklets that satisfy more customers. This 
consequently brings in more revenue and even job creation. They don’t need a couple extra 
parking spots in front of their restaurant. They need more space, and more foot traffic. 
 
The idea that the street closures hurt businesses is easily falsifiable. There are countless cities 
across the globe that have bustling economic activity on their closed streets. This includes our 
neighbors like San Carlos, and Mountain View. Even here in San Mateo, the block between 2nd 
and 3rd is hopping, especially now that the rain has relented. Our newest businesses, Somi 
Somi, and Kajiken have huge lines everyday, on the supposedly less vibrant block of B St. 
During the pandemic, Wursthall’s outdoor space was regularly jam-packed with a festive 
atmosphere despite a lack of adjacent parking. Plus, the inverse is obviously untrue; over the 
years San Mateo has seen many businesses close on car-accessible streets with ample 
parking.  
 
Retail businesses have a short shelf life, and the whims of a couple short-lived businesses 
shouldn’t dictate policy that may last for decades. We should listen to the populace: from a 
recent City of San Mateo Survey, out of 738 respondents, 82% wanted the block between 1st 
and 2nd to remain closed, and 92% indicated they would visit more often or the same amount if 
the streets remain closed.  
 
I urge you to keep B St closed to cars, and let’s see what we can do to further activate the 
walkable space on B St. 1st and 2nd, for new and existing businesses.  
 
 
Thank you, 
 
 
Jonathan New

--- page 311 ---
From: 
Martin Wiggins  
Sent on: Monday, May 15, 2023 9:24:14 PM 
To: 
City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org>; 
Clerk <clerk@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: Keep B Street Car Free, Please 
  
  
 
Dear Councilmembers, 
 
While I am a Planning Commissioner in San Mateo, I am writing as a resident and 
staunch supporter of a thriving downtown San Mateo.  I am urging you not to re-open 
B Street from 1st to 2nd to cars.  My logic is simple: 
1. For downtown to thrive as a retail environment, it needs to outcompete its 
competition, such as Hillsdale, Burlingame Ave, and Bridgepointe.   
2. To outcompete its class, it needs a competitive advantage.   
3. Car-free B Street is just such an advantage, and it offers a unique experience 
that neither visitors nor merchants can get nearby. 
4. Removing this advantage makes downtown less competitive, and less likely to 
thrive.   
5. While three blocks would've been better than two, two blocks is better than 
one, and increases the impact of our distinctive environment that we've created 
through getting cars off of this stretch of B Street. 
As such, I hope you agree that this matter has impacts far beyond one block, and 
certainly far beyond a subset of the tenants of one block.  While surveying them is no 
doubt important, it is not nearly a full picture of the stakeholder body (keeping it 
closed is broadly supported) and now is not the time to make this decision.  
 
Please think strategically about the resiliency of the symbiotic whole of downtown, 
recognize the unique character-defining feature we have, and nurture it. 
 
Thank you, 
Martin Wiggins 
 
 
-- 
Martin Wiggins

--- page 312 ---
From: 
Heather Wolnick  
Sent on: Monday, May 15, 2023 5:07:49 PM 
To: 
City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org> 
CC: 
Clerk <clerk@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: Keep B Street Closed to Cars and Open to People 
  
  
 
Dear Council, 
 
Please, please, please keep B Street between 1st & 2nd closed AND consider 
extending the pedestrian mall another block. Welcoming spaces are key to building 
community.  
 
I have to say how annoyed I am at this whole process, from the last City 
Council’s decision to make everything in the pedestrian mall temporary and 
moveable, to the onerous requirements for parklets, to the time it's taking to 
make this space NOT LOOK LIKE A STREET. Water barriers and an empty 
street might work in the short term, but it doesn’t work in the long term.  
 
We can’t give this up without actually trying it. We haven’t even made the 
space nice yet and we’re about to throw our hands up. 2 blocks forward and 1 
back. 
 
Also, the staff report should have included at least a reference to the public 
survey done. I know staff was asked by this council to survey the businesses, 
but the report is skewed without reminding everyone that an overwhelming 
majority of residents want the street to remain closed. We’re talking 11 
businesses versus 740 respondents! The sad truth is most restaurants fail in 
the first year; 70% fail within 3-5 years, with or without a closed street. So if 
we only look at the business survey here, we’re basing long-term city policy 
on the opinions of a few business owners that may not even be here next 
year, even if we do open the street to cars. 
 
Let’s not confuse correlation with causation - the businesses on one block of 
B Street may be doing poorly, but so are business all over. Blaming the closed 
street is just ridiculous, especially when everyone acknowledges the next 
block of car-free street is thriving.  
 
Some ideas to consider: 
312 of 1058

--- page 313 ---
• Planters. San Carlos came up with an ingenious way of making those water 
barriers look nice - please talk to them or refer to their latest newsletter (Public 
Works section - they are custom planters that go over the water barrier and are 
watered by the barriers themselves). They also repurposed concrete trash cans 
into planters. 
• Tables and chairs. Fill the space with places to sit. We have a ton of tables and 
chairs we haul out for events - put them there now while we designing 
everything. Some of the restaurants may want their own areas - that’s great, 
have them build them. For all the others use public dinning tables. 
• Turn this over to Parks & Rec. We have a whole department in our city that 
manages and designs public spaces people actually want to hang out in. Let's 
call this a park space instead of a street and turn it over to them. 
 
Lastly, we’re heading into summer - now is NOT the time to reopen the block 
to cars when outdoor dining demand will be at its highest. Let’s make this 
space look nice for the summer months and give it a chance. Let’s also 
expand to the block between 1st and Baldwin - we can test it this summer and 
really give it a chance.  
 
Thank you for your consideration, 
Heather Wolnick

--- page 315 ---
From: 
George California  
Sent on: Monday, May 15, 2023 10:04:59 PM 
To: 
City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org> 
Subject: Keep our Pedestrian Mall and upgrade it to a Town Square 
  
  
 
I would like to convey my strong support for maintaining and then upgrading the Pedestrian Mall 
on the entirety of B Street between 1st and 3rd Avenues.  An upgrade that should eventually 
convert a rather pedestrian Pedestrian Mall to a destination Town Square on the Peninsula.   
 
Some of my thoughts are as follows ..  
 
1) The only valid reasons to reopen B Street are to reclaim on street parking on these blocks, 
particularly for handicapped parking, and to provide direct access to storefronts by emergency 
and service vehicles.  Reopening the thoroughfare to simply provide additional or convenient 
passage for vehicles through Downtown would be highly counterproductive and a degrade of the 
quality of the Downtown environment.   
 
1a) The argument for on street parking on these blocks is spurious as the Main Street parking 
garage is located within just dozens of feet of these streets.  In fact, the garage sits right behind 
the B Street block between 1st and 2nd Avenues, and it would be just as convenient, if not more 
so, to park in the garage than on the street.   
 
1b) Configuring additional ground level parking stalls along Main Street in the parking garage 
for handicapped parking may help with the provision of adequate such parking in the area.   
 
1c) The City has already begun investigating retractable bollards for simpler service vehicle 
access to the B Street mall area.   
 
2) The setback for sidewalks along B Street is very small and growing ever overburdened as the 
City grows its Downtown Core.  The pedestrian mall alleviates this issue.  It can also provide for 
dedicated bike lanes for wheeled traffic, which would otherwise compete with pedestrians for 
limited sidewalk space, as it did before the mall was established.   
 
3) The previously overburdened sidewalk space was a safety issue.  The pedestrian mall provides 
for a much, much safer mobility environment for visitors in this area.   
 
4) The pedestrian mall, even before it eventually transforms into a robust Town Square, already 
provides an improved environment for diners, shoppers, families, bicyclists and other visitors to 
this area.   
 
5) There was discussion at the City Council when the Pedestrian Mall was being established in 
anticipation of growing pains and dislocation as the mall transitioned to its final form over an 
extended period of many years.  There was an understanding that there would be winners and 
losers during this transition period, but over the long term, there would be many more winners, 
315 of 1058

--- page 316 ---
including the City itself, with the addition of a robust traffic-free central 
open/green/recreation/entertainment space.   
 
6) Some of the losers in the short-term that were anticipated would be businesses that in some 
way rely on street parking in front of their establishments.  This seems like a very small number, 
but may be exaggerated by businesses seeking to blame failing business models on obvious, but 
dubious, given the extreme proximity of the Main Street parking garage, circumstances.   
 
6a) We should anticipate a turnover of businesses as those best fitted to a pedestrian space move 
in to replace those that do not receive a boost from the pedestrian mall.  Business turnover on B 
Street is a current reality and will probably accelerate until the right mix is found.   
 
7) We are not the first.  Pedestrian malls and town squares have revitalized city centers around 
the country and around the globe.  This is not new.  Town squares are successful developments 
dating back to antiquity.  We also don't stand alone in that we compete to be attractive to visitors 
and potential residents with neighboring cities on the Peninsula.  The pedestrian mall is not an 
improvement that leapfrogs us ahead of the competition.  It simply allows us to keep up.   
 
In summary, please maintain the entirety of the current Pedestrian Mall on B Street and work to 
expand it into the robust lively Town Square that this City deserves.   
__________________________ 
George Derby

Dear City Council Members, 
 
I am writing to express my deep concern as a property owner in the 1st-2nd block of B Street in San 
Mateo. I would like to extend my appreciation for including the discussion of the B-ST, 1st-3rd Street 
Closure on this evening's meeting agenda. The closure of S. B Street between 1st Avenue and 3rd 
Avenue for nearly two years has had a severe impact on local businesses and property owners. 
 
The closure has caused significant financial hardship for both business and property owners, leading to 
substantial revenue losses. In addition to the financial challenges posed by the pandemic, property 
owners have been forced to reduce monthly rents, while building maintenance expenses continue to rise. 
Vacancies have become increasingly prevalent, and securing new tenants has become an arduous task 
due to the street closure also, causing a loss of revenue to eh property owner.  
 
It is imperative that the City Council takes immediate action to reopen B Street between 1st and 2nd 
Avenue. Currently, there are no sit-down restaurants in the area, and the outdoor seating arrangements 
provide no discernible benefit to anyone. The presentation and placement of street closure barricades 
and traffic direction signs are inadequate the block, 1st-2nd looks like a construction zone, making it 
uninviting to the public and the community, and must be addressed promptly. 
 
While I understand the significance of the September festival and entertainment, it is crucial to consider 
the remaining eleven months of the year. I strongly believe that the business owners and property owners 
should be duly compensated by the City of San Mateo for the revenue loss resulting from the prolonged 
delay caused by the closure. Furthermore, it is essential to prioritize the functionality of the street for 
businesses throughout the entire year, rather than exclusively during the summer month. I strongly 
recommend the implementation of a rain closure pathway, rest areas, and additional parking options as 
part of the street closure project. 
 
Further delays in reopening B Street between 1st and 2nd Avenue will only exacerbate the negative 
effects on small business owners and their families, as this closure directly impacts their primary source 
of income. The early stages of the street closure process have been excessively protracted, leading to 
the impression that the best interests of businesses and property owners have been disregarded and 
overlooked. 
 
 As an alternative solution, I propose that the street closure be considered between 2nd and 3rd Avenue, 
where the majority of businesses, including sit-down restaurants and movie theaters, are located, along 
with ample open spaces that lend themselves well to such closures. 
 
I implore the City Council to prioritize this matter and take immediate action to cease the financial burden 
and reopen B-ST, 1st-2nd Street. I sincerely appreciate your consideration and prompt attention to this 
urgent issue. Please feel free to contact me by email or at 650-218-9979 if you require any additional 
information or would like to discuss this matter further. 
 
Thank you for your dedication and service to our community.

SUBJECT:
Update and Direction - Flood Relief Fund
RECOMMENDATION:
Receive an update on the Winter Storm Damage Relief Fund and provide direction to staff on how to disposition limited 
remaining funds.
BACKGROUND:
In response to the severe New Year’s Eve storms that impacted the entire region, the City Council allocated $150,000 in 
General Fund expenditures toward a new Winter Storm Damage Relief Fund. Grants of up to $5,000 were made available 
to residents and businesses that incurred damages between Dec. 31, 2022 through Jan. 12, 2023. 
Nonprofit Samaritan House was selected to administer the fund and a 30-day application period was open from March 22-
April 21, 2023. The fund was advertised through multiple channels and applications were made available in English, 
Spanish and Chinese. Samaritan House received 58 applications and determined 47 were eligible for funding. Those that 
were not eligible included five that were outside of the approved time period, and six due to incomplete applications. 
Many of the applicants who were ineligible for the City’s fund were provided alternate financial assistance through 
Samaritan House’s other funding sources. 
The total amount of relief funding distributed to the 47 eligible applicants was $178,395. In addition to the City’s $150,000 
contribution, $56,156 was contributed by donations from the Mayor’s fundraiser. After Samaritan House’s 12% 
administrative fees, there is approximately $9,761 remaining. 
As the fund was never advertised to a broader audience outside of those who incurred damages during the peak New 
Year’s Eve storm series and there is very limited funding remaining, staff recommends Council close the fund and return 
the remaining amount to the City’s General Fund. 
BUDGET IMPACT:
There is no direct fiscal impact for Council receiving this report and providing direction.
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
This administrative action is not a project subject to CEQA, because it is an organizational or administrative activity that 
will not result in direct or indirect physical changes in the environment. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(5).)

All meeting noticing requirements were met.

SUBJECT:
Climate Action Plan – Annual Progress Report
RECOMMENDATION:
Receive a progress report on the implementation of the Climate Action Plan.
BACKGROUND:
The 2020 Climate Action Plan (CAP) was adopted in April 2020 and includes strategies and measures to reduce greenhouse 
gas (GHG) emissions. The 2020 CAP contains implementation measures in the following categories: building electrification, 
renewable energy, energy efficiency, municipal energy efficiency, clean transportation fuels, sustainable transportation, 
solid waste, water and wastewater, and off-road equipment. This report provides an overview of the CAP Technical 
Update that is underway and key highlights from CAP implementation over the 2022 calendar year.
CAP Technical Update 
In addition to laying out a suite of strategies and measures to reach the City’s climate goals, the CAP was developed to 
meet a series of criteria to serve as a qualified GHG reduction strategy, which allows new development projects that are 
consistent with the CAP to streamline their environmental review. Although the CAP was recently updated and adopted, 
the City’s 2040 General Plan Update and new State laws, including AB 1279, necessitate a partial and focused technical 
update to the CAP so that the CAP will maintain consistency with the 2040 General Plan when adopted and can continue 
to serve as a qualified GHG reduction strategy. 
The 2020 CAP proposes to reduce emissions based on per-capita targets in the 2017 California Air Resources Board (CARB) 
Climate Change Scoping Plan for the years 2030 and 2050. The updated 2022 CARB Scoping Plan no longer recommends 
the use of per-capita targets for local jurisdictions and requires that 2045 be used as a threshold year, as opposed to 2050. 
Because the 2040 General Plan Update and EIR will reflect the new State laws and Bay Area Air Quality Management 
District (BAAQMD) guidance, the 2040 General Plan, once adopted, would no longer align with the CAP. It is important for 
the CAP to be consistent with the 2040 General Plan and State reduction targets because the CAP serves as a qualified 
GHG reduction strategy.
On February 21, 2023, City Council approved Agreement Amendment No. 4 to amend the scope of work with PlaceWorks 
Inc. to perform a partial and focused technical update of the CAP to ensure that it maintains consistency with the 2040 
General Plan. PlaceWorks is currently under contract with the City for the General Plan Update and is the same firm that 
updated the Climate Action Plan in 2020. This six-month project is currently underway. 
The CAP technical update will focus on updating quantitative data points, including providing a 2019 GHG inventory and 
updating GHG reduction targets and forecasts, to align with State laws and regional guidance for the new 2045 horizon 
year. This scope of work will not provide a comprehensive review and update of the CAP’s GHG reduction measures. Staff 
325 of 1058

--- page 326 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
is actively working on implementation of CAP measures, and modifications to the GHG reduction measures is not 
necessary to maintain General Plan consistency. A comprehensive update to the CAP will be evaluated as CAP 
implementation progresses. 
2019 GHG Inventory
As part of the CAP technical update, PlaceWorks completed a 2019 GHG inventory. This quantitative analysis is typically 
done every five years as it is a time-intensive process to gather the data, adjust the data based on current CAP guidance 
and methodologies, and implement the appropriate data quality assurances. The City opted for a 2019 GHG inventory as 
opposed to a 2020 or a 2021 GHG inventory so that the impact of the COVID-19 pandemic and shelter-in-place orders 
would not skew the data. The 2019 GHG inventory shows that the City reduced emissions 22% below the 2005 baseline, 
demonstrating that the City continues to exceed the 2020 goal to reduce emissions 15% below the 2005 baseline. Table 1 
summarizes the community-wide GHG emissions by sector and highlights the change in emissions from 2005 to 2019.
There were a few adjustments made to the 2005 baseline and annual inventories to reflect updated methodologies and 
thus, this inventory data is slightly different than what was included in the 2020 CAP. The inventories now include the 
transmission and distribution losses for electricity and natural gas use in the built environment, resulting to slight changes 
to the commercial/industrial built environment and residential built environment emissions. PlaceWorks also revised the 
off-road equipment emissions using the updated model from CARB and updated emission factors to maintain consistency 
with previous inventory methodologies. Additionally, the City of San Mateo has little or no control over the day-to-day 
operations of point sources. Point sources are larger institutional facilities like the San Mateo Medical Center, Mills-Peninsula 
Medical Center, and College of San Mateo that are regulated by BAAQMD. BAAQMD draft guidance on GHG reduction efforts 
recommends that the CAP should disclose these emissions, but that they can be excluded from the inventory for the 
purposes of GHG target setting and development of GHG reduction protocols. For these reasons, point sources are no longer 
included in the inventories and are instead provided as an informational item. Finally, based on new guidance, the 
PlaceWorks team is working on calculating carbon sequestration data and emissions from greenfield development to include 
in the inventories. PlaceWorks anticipates this would account for a small number of emissions and not dramatically impact 
the inventories. These data are not yet available.

GHG emissions changes from 2005 to 2019 include the following:
•
The largest reduction from 2005 to 2019 occurred in the commercial/industrial built environment sector, with 
emissions decreasing by 50%. There was also a significant reduction of 30% in emissions from the residential built 
environment sector. Emissions from the built environment have decreased since the baseline year due to a higher 
proportion of energy coming from renewable sources. Peninsula Clean Energy was introduced in 2017 and now 
provides a significant portion of San Mateo’s electricity. PG&E has also increased the portion of clean energy sources 
in its portfolio in recent years. 
•
Waste emissions have decreased year over year due to the closure of the 3rd Avenue landfill in 2000. 
•
While vehicle miles traveled (VMT) has increased year over year, emissions from on-road transportation have 
decreased slightly (3%), likely due to a higher proportion of low- and zero-emission vehicles. 
•
Emissions from the water and wastewater sector decreased 34% from 2005 levels, which is likely due to efficiency 
upgrades at the San Mateo Water Quality Control Plant. 
•
The rail sector saw a slight increase, with emissions increasing by 2%, which is driven by an increase in Caltrain 
ridership. 
•
Emissions from point sources increased 145% from 2005 to 2019, potentially as a result of changes in business 
operations or how data are tracked. These are emission sources that are often outside the City’s jurisdiction. 
PlaceWorks is now working on preparing forecasts of future GHG emissions for the years 2030, 2040, and 2045 and updating 
the GHG reduction benefits from existing and planned State activities through 2045. 
Annual Progress Report Key Highlights
To track CAP implementation, staff provides annual progress reports to the Sustainability and Infrastructure Commission 
and City Council on the status of each implementation measure. The annual update was presented to the Sustainability 
and Infrastructure Commission at their April 11, 2023 meeting. Attachment 1 to this report provides both qualitative and 
quantitative data to show how progress has been made on each of the CAP measures. Some key metrics highlighting 
implementation of the 2020 CAP are as follows: 
•
Building permits issued for all-electric residential buildings in 2022: 4 single family homes, 1 duplex, and 25 
Accessory Dwelling Units
•
Estimated total GHGs avoided by Peninsula Clean Energy electricity in 2022: 54,922 MTCO2e
•
Total kW of solar permits issued for residential buildings in 2022: 3,383 kW
•
Individual rebate measures through BayREN Home + program in 2022: 101 individual measure rebates
•
Electricity and gas savings from PG&E Sustainable Solutions Turnkey energy efficiency upgrades at municipal 
facilities in 2022: 1,808,919 kWh and 28,624 therms
•
Publicly available electric vehicle charging ports in the City at the close of 2022: 136 electric vehicle charging ports 
•
Miles of bike lanes installed in 2022: 2.79 miles
•
Organics diverted in 2022: 19,004 tons
•
Water savings as a result of conservation programs in Cal Water’s Mid-Peninsula District (includes San Mateo and 
San Carlos) in 2022: 10.5 million gallons
Other key highlights include:
•
Building electrification: In November 2022, the City Council adopted reach codes requiring that the construction 
of all new buildings be all-electric, expanding the City’s previous requirement requiring all-electric construction of 
327 of 1058

--- page 328 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
new residential and office buildings. In addition, the City Council adopted reach codes impacting residential 
building upgrades to encourage electric-readiness and require electric equipment. The City of San Mateo is one of 
a handful of cities with electrification requirements for existing residential buildings. In recent news, on March 15, 
2023, the Bay Area Air Quality Management District (BAAQMD) adopted rules to phase out the sale and 
installation of gas water heaters by 2027 and gas furnaces by 2029. Electric panel upgrades are often identified as 
a barrier for replacing gas equipment with electric. The City’s reach code that requires breaker space and panel 
capacity for future electric equipment will help prepare homeowners for future electric equipment requirements.
•
Induction Cooktop Loaner Program: In June 2022, the City launched the Induction Cooktop Loaner Program that 
offers hands-on experience with a portable induction cooktop. Residents can borrow an induction cooktop and 
cookware for two weeks. 15 residents borrowed the induction cooktop kit in 2022. 
•
Traffic Signal Optimization: In 2022, the City implemented traffic signal optimizations on the Fashion Island 
Boulevard corridor from Delaware Street to Norfolk Street, and on a short segment of Humboldt Street from 3rd 
Avenue to 4th Avenue. Traffic signal optimizations help reduce vehicle emissions related to vehicle idling.
•
Complete Streets Plan: The City received $490,882 in grant funding for the development of a citywide Complete 
Streets Plan. The outcome will be an actionable plan with a list of prioritized multimodal infrastructure projects 
that strive to provide safe, reliable, and accessible travel options for all modes. The City procured a consultant to 
assist with developing the plan and anticipates community engagement beginning in spring 2023.
•
Disposable Food Service Ware Ordinance: The City Council adopted the “Disposable Food Service Ware 
Ordinance” modeled after the County of San Mateo’s ordinance. The ordinance requires food facilities distribute 
disposable items that are fiber-based and compostable. The ordinance went into effect in October 2022. In the 
months leading up to October 2022, the County of San Mateo Office of Sustainability spearheaded outreach and 
engagement with San Mateo food facilities. On August 16, 2022, the County hosted a workshop for food facilities 
to showcase compliant food ware and meet food ware vendors. 332 people attended the event. The County’s 
consultant also contacted 553 businesses in the City through phone calls, emails and in-person visits and made 
direct contact with decision makers at 62% of those businesses to provide information. This included outreach and 
education in Spanish and Chinese where appropriate. 
•
OKAPI Reusables: The City of San Mateo is partnering with OKAPI Reusables, thanks to leadership and funding 
from the County of San Mateo Office of Sustainability. OKAPI is a simple borrow and return cup service featuring 
trendy insulated stainless-steel cups for hot drinks and glass tumblers for iced drinks. 3 Bees Coffee (224 E. 3rd 
Avenue), Urban Ritual (140 B Street), Meet Fresh (227 S. B Street) and Heere Tea (253 S. B Street) are the first 
cafes to join the OKAPI Reusables network. The program launched in December 2022. 
•
Cal Water’s Climate Efforts: Cal Water completed a Climate Change Risk Assessment & Adaptation Framework in 
December 2021. The report identifies and prioritizes climate-driven risks to Cal Water facilities, operations, and 
water supply portfolio. It also projects and assesses changes to water supply and demand. This will allow Cal Water 
to more effectively develop adaptation strategies that reduce the impact of climate change on Cal Water’s 
operations, prioritize infrastructure investments that increase resiliency, and monitor and address risks to 
disadvantaged and vulnerable communities. 
•
Rain barrel rebates: The City had a record number of 82 rain barrel rebates distributed in 2022.
On April 17, 2023, the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals invalidated the City of Berkeley’s ordinance to prohibit new natural 
gas infrastructure. San Mateo and at least 50 other jurisdictions throughout California have required all-electric 
construction through amendments to the Building Code (reach codes). Staff will continue to monitor developments in the 
law.

There is no fiscal impact associated with this administrative action.

BUILDING ELECTRIFICATION
BE 1 ALL-ELECTRIC NEW CONSTRUCTION

•
All-electric construction during the 2019 Code Cycle (January 1, 2020 – December 31, 2022)
o

BE 2 ALL-ELECTRIC EXISTING BUILDINGS
•
On March 15, 2023, the Bay Area Air Quality Management District (BAAQMD) adopted rules to 
phase out the sale and installation of gas water heaters by 2027 and gas furnaces by 2029. 
•
In November 2022, the City Council adopted reach codes impacting residential building upgrades 
to encourage electric-readiness and require electric equipment. These reach codes went into 
effect on January 1, 2023 and are summarized as follows:
o
Electric readiness panel capacity: Requires panel replacement and panel upgrade 
projects to include panel capacity and breaker space for future electrification of building 
systems for all residential buildings.
o
Electric-readiness outlets installed: Requires kitchen and laundry area renovations 
include the installation of an outlet for the use of future electric appliances in single 
family homes and duplexes.
o
Heat pump HVAC: Requires the installation of heat pump HVAC system when new air 
conditioning is installed ore replaced in single family homes and duplexes.
o
Gas infrastructure: Prohibits the extension of fuel gas infrastructure for new uses such 
as new fireplaces, fire pits, grills and pools in residential buildings.
o
Heat pump water heater: Requires heat pump water heater installation in addition and 
alteration projects that include water heater replacement in single family homes and 
duplexes.
•
Through the BayREN Home + Program, residents can receive rebates for the installation of

2
electric equipment including, heat pump water heaters (HPWH), heat pump dryers or induction

7 electric panel upgrades. In 2022, PCE distributed rebates for 24 HPWHs, 7 heat pump HVAC 
installations, and 6 electric panel upgrades. Note, San Mateo residents can layer rebates from 
both PCE and BayREN.
•
In June 2022, the City launched the Induction Cooktop Loaner Program that offers hands-on 
experience with a portable induction cooktop. Residents can borrow an induction cooktop and 
cookware for two weeks. In 2022, 15 residents borrowed the induction cooktop kit.
