ENERGY EFFICIENCY
EE 1 RESIDENTIAL ENERGY EFFICIENCY RETROFITS
•
The BayREN Home + program provides rebates for energy efficiency upgrades including building 
air sealing, insulation, duct replacement and sealing, and more. In 2020, 16 single family home 
upgrades were completed, and 41 individual measure rebates issued. In 2021, 21 single family 
home upgrades were completed, and 107 individual measure rebates issued. In 2022, 44 single 
family home upgrades were completed, and 101 individual measure rebates issued. 
•
The BayREN Home Energy Advisors are a resource to residents interested in making energy 
efficiency improvements. In 2020, 44 new Energy Advisor leads were generated and in 2021 
there were a total of 72 leads. In 2022 there were 60 leads generated. 
•
The Bay Area Multifamily Building Enhancements (BAMBE) program provides rebates for 
332 of 1058

--- page 333 ---
CAP PROGRESS REPORT - 2023
4
building energy savings; measures include heating and cooling systems, water heating systems, 
lighting and appliances, building sealing and insulation and more. In 2020, 7 units participated in 
the program and in 2021, 18 units participated in the program. In 2022, 0 units participated in 
the program. 
•
The Home Energy Score program provides a score of home energy use on a scale of 1 to 10. It 
estimates home energy use, associated costs, and provides energy solutions to cost-effectively 
improve home efficiency. In 2020, there were 77 home energy score rebates and in 2021, there 
were 44 home energy score rebates in San Mateo. In 2022, there were 26 home energy score 
rebates in San Mateo.
•
PCE’s Home Upgrade program serves income-qualified homeowners with home repairs and 
energy efficiency upgrades at no cost. Eligible improvements include door and window repair, 
water heating, heating and cooling, cooking, and more. In 2021, PCE’s Home Upgrade program 
served 3 households and in 2022, PCE’s Home Upgrade program served 8 households. 
EE 2 NONRESIDENTIAL ENERGY EFFICIENCY RETROFITS
•
There are 19 certified businesses in San Mateo certified through the San Mateo County Green 
Business Program. The program includes energy efficiency measures along with other 
sustainable business practices.
EE 3 RESIDENTIAL TREE PLANTING
•
In addition to tree plantings in City parks, 150 trees are planted in City streets annually. Not all 
tree plantings are in residential areas but nonetheless help reduce the urban heat island effect 
and reduce cooling loads.
MUNICIPAL ENERGY EFFICENCY
ME 1 ENERGY EFFICIENCY FOR NEW BUILDINGS
•
The construction of Fire Station 25 is complete. Fire Station 25 includes the installation of 10 kW 
of solar PV that exceeds the minimum required by the City’s reach code.
•
The San Mateo Wastewater Treatment Plant is pursuing certification for the Administration 
Building under LEED v4 for New Construction. The project is targeting a minimum LEED Silver 
certification. The project includes a 25 kW PV system on the roof of the Administration Building 
and installation of five electric vehicle chargers.

ME 3 ALL-ELECTRIC MUNICIPAL BUILDINGS
•
The City is part of a public/private partnership to construct a new residential building and parking 
garage on two City-owned parcels Downtown. The seven-story affordable housing project, 
known as Kiku Crossing, will be all-electric. The new five-story parking garage will also be all-
electric and include the installation of 27 Level 2 electric vehicle chargers to comply with the 
City’s reach code requirements for EV infrastructure. A solar system will be installed on the 
parking garage to meet and exceed the City’s solar reach code requirement. Construction is 
underway.
•
The City participated in a critical facilities assessment with Peninsula Clean Energy (PCE) to 
identify facilities to install solar and battery storage that might serve as a possible resilient site 
in the event of grid outages. Through this process, the City provided facility data to PCE for 
review and assessment. The San Mateo Police Department was selected as a site with potential 
opportunity, PCE and consultant staff toured the site. Due to the need for major roof repairs, 
the installation of solar at the Police Department site is currently on hold.
334 of 1058

--- page 335 ---
CAP PROGRESS REPORT - 2023
6
•
In 2022, PCE expanded their Solar and Storage for Public Buildings program and is working with 
jurisdictions across the region to identify more sites for solar installation. PCE will provide energy 
and savings analyses, vendor selection via public solicitation, management, and facilitation of a 
Power Purchase Agreement (PPA). A PPA would provide the City with a fixed price for electricity 
without any capital outlay. Staff are coordinating with PCE to identify additional opportunities 
for solar at City-owned sites. 
CLEAN TRANSPORTATION FUELS
CF 1 ELECTRIC VEHICLE CHARGING INFRASTRUCTURE
•
In April 2022, the City completed installation of 13 Level 2 EV charging ports at the Main Street 
Garage in Downtown San Mateo. The project includes the installation of Powerflex equipment 
utilizing CALeVIP rebate funding, greatly reducing project capital costs. 
•
In July 2022, CALeVIP notified City staff that the rebate application for the 2nd and El Camino 
Garage had been approved. Staff negotiated an agreement for the design and installation of 14 
Level 2 EV charging ports with Powerflex. The project is anticipated to close out in April 2023.
•
There are 31 EV charging ports at City-owned facilities, this includes 2 DCFC charging ports and 
29 Level 2 charging ports. Additionally, there are 136 charging ports that are publicly available 
in the city; this includes 14 DCFC charging ports, 90 Level 2 charging ports and 32 Tesla charging 
ports.
•
In 2022, the City adopted a reach code requiring a higher percentage of electric vehicle (EV) 
readiness in all new construction. These requirements went into effect on January 1, 2023.

SUSTAINABLE TRANSPORTATION
ST 1 BICYCLE MODE SHARE
•
The City was awarded $1.66M from the 2021 Regional ATP Cycle 5 to fund the design and 
construction of a Safe Routes to School corridor comprised of a new Class IV separated bike lane 
and bicycle boulevard on Delaware Street from 19th Avenue to Pacific Boulevard. The City is in 
the process of procuring a consultant to assist with the project.
•
The City received $153,000 of grant funding to design a new bicycle facility on 28th Avenue from 
Edison Street to the Caltrain Station to close a gap in the bicycle network and connect bicyclists 
to the Caltrain station at 28th Avenue. Based on the feedback from the walk audit conducted in 
February 2022 with transit agencies and City staff, the project team is working on expanding the 
scope of the project to Delaware Street to develop a bicycle facility that connects bicyclists to 
the Caltrain Station and provides east-west connectivity on 28th Avenue.
•
The City was awarded $200,000 in ACR/TDM grant program funding, $2M in TA Pedestrian & 
Bicycle Cycle 6 funding, and $3.375M in grant funding from the One Bay Area Grant (OBAG) 3 to 
implement Complete Streets infrastructure along 19th Avenue/Fashion Island Boulevard. The 
project includes construction of a two-way Class IV separated bicycle facility, pedestrian access 
improvements, such as high visibility crosswalks and curb extensions, at four intersections along 
the corridor, and green infrastructure improvements. This effort is a joint project with the San 
Mateo County Transportation Authority and SamTrans.
•
The City submitted a successful proposal to participate in the 2021-22 San Mateo County of 
Education (SMCOE) School Travel Fellowship to evaluate pedestrian and bicycle safety near San 
Mateo High School and College Park Elementary School. Feedback from a walk audit conducted 
in March 2022 with school representatives, parents, and SMCOE and City staff was used to 
develop improvements around the schools, including flashing beacons, signal changes, and 
crosswalk improvements. 
•
The City completed construction of Class II bike lanes and a bicycle boulevard on Poplar Avenue, 
Delaware Street, Indian Avenue, and Humboldt Street in May 2022. These are the 2nd and 4th 
highest priority projects in the 2020 Bicycle Master Plan.
•
The City constructed Class II bike lanes on Humboldt Street and Pacific Avenue and a bicycle 
boulevard on 5th Avenue from Delaware Street to Amphlett Boulevard in Summer 2022 in 
coordination with the City’s Pavement Management Program.
•
In 2021 2.79 miles of bike facilities were constructed and in 2022, 3.63 miles of bike facilities 
were constructed. There is a total of ~62.4 miles of bike facilities.
ST 2 PEDESTRIAN MODE SHARE
•
The City received $490,882 in grant funding for the development of a citywide Complete Streets 
Plan. The outcome will be an actionable plan with a list of prioritized multimodal infrastructure 
projects that strive to provide safe, reliable, and accessible travel options for all modes. The City 
procured a consultant to assist with developing the Plan and anticipates community engagement 
beginning in Spring 2023.
•
The City’s Transit-Oriented Development Pedestrian Access Plan was adopted by the City Council 
in November 2022. Data analysis and community input were combined to develop a list of capital 
337 of 1058

--- page 338 ---
CAP PROGRESS REPORT - 2023
9
improvement projects that aim to improve pedestrian conditions within one-half mile radius of 
the City’s Caltrain three stations and high-quality transit stops. 
ST 3 MICROMOBILITY AND SHARED MOBILITY 
•
The City updated its shared mobility permit program to allow shared mobility operators to 
submit applications on an on-going basis. No applications were received when the program was 
initially launched in 2019. The permit program continues to be available to interested parties.
•
Staff continues to participate in a Shared Mobility Working Groups coordinated by San Mateo 
County and Caltrain to facilitate regional partnerships and share best practices as related to 
shared mobility. 
ST 4 PUBLIC TRANSIT SERVICE
•
City staff participated in the Reimagine SamTrans effort and the El Camino Real Bus Speed Study 
to assess existing transit service and evaluate options for future mobility services.
•
In 2020, City staff worked with SamTrans to implement signal priority on El Camino Real through 
the length of the City.
ST 5 COMMUTER PROGRAMS
•
San Mateo County’s Transportation Demand Management Agency, Commute.org maintains a 
suite of programs aimed at reducing vehicle miles traveled. Commute.org provides carpool 
incentives to employers and conducts outreach campaigns about available shuttle and vanpool 
options.
•
Commute.org has maintained shuttle services in San Mateo including the Campus Drive shuttle 
route. The City contributes funding to Commute.org shuttles for the Campus Drive shuttle route.
ST 6 TRANSPORTATION DEMAND MANAGEMENT
•
The City was awarded grant funding to develop a citywide Transportation Demand Management 
ordinance. City staff kicked off the project and anticipate engagement with stakeholders to start 
in Spring 2023. Final adoption is anticipated in Spring 2024.

SW 1 COMPOSTING PROGRAM
•
In 2021, 498 multifamily dwellings with 5 units or more out of 730 multifamily buildings have

SW 2 EXPANDED RECYCLING SERVICE
•
Recology reported no rejected loads of organic or recycling materials due to contamination. The 
current levels of contamination threshold per the franchise agreement is not to exceed 8% for 
recycling, 10% for commercial organics and 5% for residential organics.
SW 3 WASTE AWARENESS AND SOURCE REDUCTION
•
The City Council adopted the “Disposable Food Service Ware Ordinance” modeled after the 
County of San Mateo’s ordinance. The ordinance requires food facilities distribute disposable 
items that are fiber-based and compostable. The ordinance went into effect in October 2022. In 
the months leading up to October 2022, the County of San Mateo Office of Sustainability 
spearheaded outreach and engagement with San Mateo food facilities. On August 16, 2022 the 
County hosted a workshop for food facilities to showcase compliant food ware and meet food 
ware vendors. 332 people attended the event. The County’s consultant also contacted 553 
businesses in the city through phone calls, emails and in-person visits and made direct contact 
with decision makers at 62% of those businesses to provide information. This included outreach 
and education in Spanish and Chinese where appropriate. 
•
The City of San Mateo is partnering with OKAPI Reusables, thanks to leadership and funding from 
the County of San Mateo Office of Sustainability. OKAPI is a simple borrow and return cup service 
featuring trendy insulated stainless steel cups for hot drinks and glass tumblers for iced drinks. 
3 Bees Coffee (224 E. 3rd Avenue), Urban Ritual (140 B Street), Meet Fresh (227 S. B Street) and

Heere Tea (253 S. B Street) are the first cafes to join the OKAPI Reusables network. The program 
launched in December 2022.
•
Team Up To Clean Up brings San Mateo residents and neighborhoods together in a joint effort 
to eliminate litter in our streets, storm drains, parks, and waterways. Our City program organizes 
monthly cleanups in areas in need of cleaning up. Our program also teams up with the County’s 
Annual Bay Front Cleanup initiative. In 2020, the program had 74 participants contributing 204 
hours of volunteer service and in 2021 the program had 982 participants contributing 3,140 
hours of volunteer service. In 2022, the program had 880 participants contributing 2,400 hours 
of volunteer service.

WW 2 WATER EFFICIENT LANDSCAPING
•
Cal Water provides Smart Landscape Tune-Up Direct Installation Program Evaluations. Through 
this program irrigation system efficiency evaluations were conducted by professional landscape 
contractors to customers. Irrigation system improvements included leak repairs and installation 
of a smart irrigation controller and/or high-efficiency sprinkler nozzles. The Program is available 
at no charge to customers.  
•
Cal Water provide rebates for weather-based irrigation controllers were offered as part of Cal 
Water's standard rebate program portfolio and as a direct installation through the Smart 
Landscape Tune-Up Program. 
•
Through Cal Water’s Spray-to-Drip Rebate Program, eligible participants received a rebate of 
$0.50 for every square foot of landscape area converted from a standard spray irrigation system 
to a drip irrigation system. The program requires a minimum of 250 square feet to be converted 
to a drip system. Drip irrigation uses 20-50% less water than spray irrigation and delivers water 
directly to the root zone of plants.
•
Through the “Lawn-to-garden” (turf replacement) rebate program, Cal Water incentivized 
customers to replace their lawn with drought-tolerant and water-wise landscaping with a rebate 
of up to $3 and $5 per square foot for residential and commercial customers, respectively. Cal 
Water provides rebates for smart irrigation controllers and high-efficiency sprinkler nozzles for 
residential customers.

•
Cal Water estimates that conservation programs used by customers in Cal Water’s Mid Peninsula 
District (includes San Mateo and San Carlos) resulted in an annual savings of 10.5 million gallons 
in 2022, 3.6 million gallons in 2021, and 2 million gallons in 2020.
•
Cal Water completed a Climate Change Risk Assessment & Adaptation Framework in December 
2021. The report identifies and prioritizes climate-driven risks to Cal Water facilities, operations, 
and water supply portfolio. It also projects and assesses changes to water supply and demand. 
This will allow Cal Water to more effectively develop adaptation strategies that reduce the 
impact of climate change on Cal Water’s operations, prioritize infrastructure investments that 
increase resiliency, and monitor and address risks to disadvantaged and vulnerable 
communities.
•
Parks made significant landscape improvements at Beresford Park along Alameda de las Pulgas 
and at the Senior Center to reduce water use by replacing spray heads with bubbler irrigation 
and adding mulch to areas to conserve moisture.
341 of 1058

--- page 342 ---
CAP PROGRESS REPORT - 2023
13
•
Parks has placed hundreds of yards wood chips to reduce weed pressure in bare areas, 
conserving moisture for trees, and diverting the tree trimmings from the waste stream.
•
City staff ensure compliance with the State of California’s Water Efficient Landscape Ordinance 
(WELO) that applies to rehabilitated landscape projects with landscape area greater than 1000 
square feet. This ordinance was created to reduce water usage and ensure landscapes are 
constructed to allow for the efficient use of water.
WW 3 WATER EFFICIENCY IN NEW CONSTRUCTION
•
City staff ensure compliance with the State of California’s Water Efficient Landscape Ordinance 
(WELO) that applies to all new construction projects with landscape area greater than 500 
square feet. This ordinance was created to reduce water usage and ensure landscapes are 
constructed to allow for the efficient use of water.

Staff purchased a variety of electric equipment (backpack leaf blowers, handheld leaf blowers,

life and charging infrastructure are still challenges and electric equipment cannot fully replace 
the use of gas-powered blowers at this time. Staff is in the process of identifying sweeping 
equipment capable of picking up debris on paths and parking lots to reduce dependency on 
backpack blowers. The Parks Department is requesting a budget allocation for the new multi-
purpose sweeper equipment in 2023. 
•
In October 2021, California became the first state with a statewide ban on new gas leaf blowers 
and lawn mowers when Governor Newsom signed Assembly Bill 1346 prohibiting the sale of new 
small off-road engines beginning January 1, 2024.

SUBJECT:
Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion Strategic Framework – Adoption
RECOMMENDATION:
Approve the Citywide Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion (DEI) Strategic Framework, as developed by staff, to establish a DEI 
lens for City operations.
BACKGROUND:
The City prides itself on being a vibrant and diverse community, one that is committed to inclusivity and equity. Furthering 
that commitment, the City Council prioritized the formation of a Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion (DEI) Task Force as a 
strategic initiative in their fiscal year 2021-22 Strategic Plan. The DEI Task Force is comprised of representatives from the 
City’s partner agencies, local non-profits, and neighborhood organizations. During the initial Task Force meeting in January 
2022, members of the Task Force expressed concern that the City does not have a strategic vision for guiding DEI-related 
efforts throughout the City. The Task Force recommended staff develop a framework for how to incorporate DEI into City 
operations. 
After receiving approval from City Council to develop a DEI Strategic Plan in April 2022, staff began researching equity 
plans adopted by various cities for inspiration and to learn best practices. Staff also reached out to various DEI 
practitioners and consultants who could assist in developing a Citywide DEI Strategic Plan.  After completion of an informal 
bid process, staff hired CenterFour Consulting to assist in the creation of the DEI Strategic Framework that would articulate 
the City’s vision, goals, and guiding principles to demonstrate how the City will incorporate DEI into City operations and to 
evaluate outcomes to ensure that intentions are met.
The Strategic Plan effort was led by a steering committee, which included the Assistant City Manager, Management 
Analysts from the City Manager’s Office and Community Development Department, the City Librarian, and the strategic 
planning consultant. In July 2022, the steering committee formed an internal staff working group, made up of 13 staff 
members from key operational departments including Community Development, Parks and Recreation, Library, Public 
Works, Human Resources, and Police. The internal working group followed a 3-step module process to create a DEI 
Strategic Framework that consisted of a vision statement, guiding principles, goals, and criteria for development and 
evaluation. The internal working group met seven times from July to October 2022 to complete each of the modules. The 
modules and outcomes are described below: 
Module One focused on the creation of the vision statement, guiding principles, and goals for the Strategic Framework.  
The vision statement keeps the City’s aspirations front and center. The guiding principles ensure the City’s values are 
maintained in the long term. The goals give specific direction and targeted aims for City staff to follow. In addition, as part 
343 of 1058

--- page 344 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
of Module One, staff prepared a demographic analysis of the community that demonstrated the diversity within it in terms 
of age, racial background, languages spoken, income levels, and housing tenure.
Module Two established criteria questions intended to ensure a DEI lens is applied to current and future planning of 
programs, projects, and policies. This serves as a tool for staff to ensure DEI considerations are in the forefront for the 
creation of new initiatives. 
Module Three established a measurement/evaluation criterion for implementation teams to gauge the impact and 
relevance of their work. This serves as a tool for implementation teams to ensure initiatives have the intended impact and 
achieve the goals set forth in Module One.
Once the content for each of the modules was completed, staff presented the draft outcome to the DEI Task Force to 
review and comment to provide input from the community perspective. The internal working group utilized the feedback 
and expertise shared by the Task Force to refine the content for each of the modules. The DEI Task Force met to review 
and comment on the DEI Strategic Framework a total of five times. 
From January to March 2023, the consultant worked on creating Strategic Framework report using the content created 
from the modules and feedback from the internal working group and the DEI Task Force. The final draft report was then 
reviewed by the internal working group on March 17, 2023, and the external DEI Task Force on April 21, 2023.  Both 
groups recommended that the framework be presented to City Council for adoption. 
Following the adoption of the DEI Strategic Framework, staff will work to integrate the framework into our daily 
operations. In addition to applying the framework to new projects, programs, and policies, it is important to also evaluate 
the work currently underway. In order to accomplish this, staff plans to retain CenterFour consulting to facilitate 
discussions with each City department to audit existing programs and policies, using the criteria from Module 3. The 
outcomes of these departmental audits will create a work plan for actionable refinements to existing programs and 
policies. These recommended changes will be shared with the DEI Task Force for review and comment. 
BUDGET IMPACT:
The City budgeted $50,000 for facilitation services to assist with the development of the DEI Strategic Framework. Staff 
plans to continue working with a facilitator to assist City departments with the implementation of the framework. This cost 
is already included in the City Manager’s Office budget.
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
The adoption of the DEI Strategic Framework is not a project subject to CEQA, because it is a government administrative 
activity which does not involve any commitment to any specific project which may result in a potentially significant 
physical impact on the environment. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(4).)
NOTICE PROVIDED
All meeting noticing requirements were met.

3
Executive Summary
The City of San Mateo is committed to making diversity, equity, and inclusion (DEI) 
a top priority in our community. During the midst of the pandemic and economic 
recession, we were confronted with the legacy of the lack of progress we as a nation 
have made in addressing discrimination in our society and institutions. San Mateo is 
choosing not to accept this legacy, and we will work toward becoming better allies 
and change makers for our colleagues, our communities, and beyond. We know 
collective action is needed to effect systematic change and create a more just society 
for all community members. 
This Citywide DEI Strategic Plan Framework is our first step in creating meaningful 
and long-lasting positive change. It is a guidance document that encourages 
constructive and realistic approaches toward a higher standard of inclusion. 
And most impressively, it accounts for the need to work together as both service 
providers and neighbors.
We put together a team of professionals to address this vitally important 
undertaking, including City staff and our Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion Task Force 
which is comprised of representatives from our partner agencies, local non-profits, 
and neighborhood organizations. I want to personally thank every one of the 
participants who contributed to building this framework. They represent the many 
faces of San Mateo and all aspects of our city and city government. Their unique and 
informed perspectives helped to set a new standard toward meaningful equity and 
inclusion in our City. 
Drew Corbett, City Manager

Introduction to the 
DEI Strategic Framework Report
Every member of the City of San Mateo community should feel a sense of belonging. 
The Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion, (DEI) Strategic Framework shared in this report 
is twofold: it informs community members of our commitment to building and 
supporting that sense of belonging; and it provides guidance for those charged with 
integrating DEI awareness into our programs, projects, and policies. This work is 
inspired by the vision in our City’s General Plan and refined by our DEI Internal Staff 
Committee. 
As a precursor to the creation of this citywide DEI Strategic Framework our DEI 
Internal Staff Committee provided the following definitions:
Diversity - All the ways in which people differ.
Equity - Fair treatment, access, opportunity, and advancement for all people. To 
create conditions that allow all to reach their full potential.  
One’s identity cannot predict the outcome.
Inclusion - A variety of people have power, a voice, and decision-making authority.
San Mateo is a vibrant, livable, diverse, and healthy community 
that respects the quality of its neighborhoods, fosters a flourishing 
economy, is committed to equity, and is a leader in environmental 
sustainability. We embrace diversity and respect the experiences, 
contributions, and aspirations of people of all ages, abilities, 
incomes, and backgrounds. We strive to include everyone in 
community life and decisions for a shared, sustainable future. 
— Excerpt from the City of San Mateo General Plan Vision Statement

Who is San Mateo? We are San Mateo
In San Mateo today, more than one language is spoken in nearly half of all 
households. And while many residents are highly educated and prosperous, there 
are many struggling to escape poverty and to provide for their families. The numbers 
illustrate the need to provide services with a lens for equity versus equality. Equality 
provides everyone with the same solutions. Equity 
creates solutions according to unique needs.

6
Our Approach to Creating 
a Strategic Framework
Imagine going on a trip to a new place without a map, fly-
ing a plane without a flight plan, or preparing a city for its 
future without an intentional strategy. Integrating a lens of 
diversity, equity, and inclusion into our city operations re-
quires strategic thinking and action. And it all begins with the 
development of a plan. The purpose of this report is to both docu-
ment our approach to creating a strategic framework, and to provide a road map for 
the implementation of a DEI lens to all current and future city operations. 
The strategic planning team reviewed DEI plans from cities around the country to 
use as inspiration. The team implemented a three-pronged approach to building a 
strategic framework, which included: 
Setting up the pillars of the DEI Strategic Framework 
Creating criteria for a DEI lens to be applied to current and future 
planning of programs, projects, and policies
Developing measurement/evaluation criteria for implementation teams to 
gauge the impact and relevance of their work 
The City of San Mateo DEI Task Force reviewed each element of the strategic 
framework and provided input from the community perspective.
350 of 1058

--- page 351 ---
7
Overview of the Strategic Framework 
Each aspect of the DEI Strategic Framework provides implementation teams with guidance 
for operationalizing Diversity, Equity, and Inclusion using the Strategic Pillars. It is important 
to note that this is a living document which provides flexibility for implementation teams to 
tailor the suggested approaches to fit their areas of responsibility. 
The Vision Statement keeps the City’s aspirations front and center. The Guiding Principles 
ensure that City cultural values are maintained in the long term, and the Goals give specific 
direction or targeted aims. 
Critical Questions serve as a tool for teams to make timely and accurate assessments in 
the creation of initiatives, as well as in the measurement and evaluation of those initiatives 
to help ensure they have the intended impact.
Vision Statement
The City of San Mateo honors and welcomes diversity and is a place where 
all unique perspectives are heard and valued, a community of equitable 
opportunities and outcomes for all.
Guiding Principles
•	 We place equity and inclusion 
at the forefront of all city services, 
policies, and programs
•	 We cultivate a community of 
respect with an appreciation for 
diverse perspectives 
•	 We stand united against hate
•	 We aspire to have full community 
engagement 
•	 We strive to ensure that all residents 
have an excellent Quality of Life
•	 We both honor and acknowledge 
our past as we welcome new ideas 
and cultures
•	 We prioritize the undoing of 
institutional Racial Oppression
Goals
Institutionalize equity and racial 
justice into City policies, processes, 
and programs by improving 
accessibility and removing barriers
Empower diverse and inclusive 
perspectives by connecting, 
collaborating, and building trust with 
underserved communities
Proactively address inequity by 
undoing all forms of systemic or 
environmental bias, discrimination, 
and harassment, which impact quality 
of life
The Pillars of the DEI Strategic Framework
351 of 1058

--- page 352 ---
8
Critical questions are an effective way to 
establish criteria for current and future initiatives 
and to evaluate their impact. The strategic 
planning team created critical questions to 
further define the tenets of a DEI lens. 
Criteria for 
Implementation 
and Evaluation  
of DEI Initiatives
Criteria for the Development of Programs, Projects, 
and Policies
Understanding the Need or Purpose
What is the purpose of what we are making/implementing? 
•	 What is the historical context for this purpose? 
•	 What are we trying to accomplish through this project/service/policy?
•	 What issues are we trying to address?
What does success look like for this effort? 
•	 How will success be measured? 
•	 How will we know when success is reached?
Program/Policy Impacts
Who is being served? Is there a target population? 
What are the potential impacts of this project/service/policy? 
•	 Are there any adverse impacts or unintended consequences that could be an outcome 
of the project/service/policy? 
•	 Does it benefit one group at the expense of another?
What are the potential barriers to access the benefits of the service/project?  
Examples include: 
•	 Language? 
•	 Citizenship status? 
•	 Income? 
•	 Complexity of required paperwork? Is access internet-only? 
•	 Awareness of the program/policy/or service? 
•	 Disabilities?
•	 Limited availability/access? 
•	 Technology?
•	 Transportation limitations?
•	 Historical distrust from marginalized communities?
352 of 1058

--- page 353 ---
History has taught us that broad brush initiatives, although well-
intentioned, can bring disparate outcomes. A city holds its own 
unique collection of neighborhoods and diverse communities. 
And as with the difference between equity and equality, it is 
crucial that strategic plans acknowledge the unique needs and 
cultures within a community to achieve desired outcomes.
Are we duplicating efforts of other City Departments, outside agencies, or non-
profits? 
•	 What are the opportunities for collaboration?
Is there an opportunity to pilot the program/project before making it permanent? 
What data/evidence (whether qualitative or quantitative) can we use to make 
informed decisions? 
Was geographic equity considered?
Communication 
Did you engage with the target population as part of the development process for 
the proposed project/service/policy? 
Did you engage with adversely impacted communities as part of the development 
process? 
How will the availability of this program/service/policy be communicated to the 
impacted populations once it is being implemented? 
What are the potential barriers to communication (such as language or access to 
the method of communication) and what is the plan to address them? 
Have you built enough time and budget into the project to allow for outreach and 
communication?
9
353 of 1058

--- page 354 ---
After Implementation Evaluation Criteria
Understanding the Need or Purpose
Did the project/program/policy succeed in meeting the anticipated outcome?
•	 Are there any metrics available to support this finding?
•	 What methods other than metrics can illustrate the resulting outcome and what is 
your baseline for measurement?
Were there any unforeseen outcomes (positive or negative)?
•	 What did you learn from any negative unforeseen outcomes?
•	 Did your process include options to pivot in case unforeseen outcomes arise? 
Program/Policy/Projects Impacts (for use if applicable)
Was the enrollment/uptake what was expected in terms of total quantity?
•	 If not, what were the potential barriers and how can they be addressed?
•	 If oversubscribed, is there capacity to expand or to be more targeted about 
participation?
•	 Did people inquire but not follow through? Do we know why?
Did we reach the intended population?
•	 Were any groups under or over-represented? (By age, ethnicity, language, etc.)
•	 Were there any notable differences in which groups inquired but did not follow 
through?
Has there been consistent participation, or has it dropped since the initial launch? 
Are there geographic equity indicators?
Communication
How did people learn about the project/program/policy?
•	 Did the outcome match with the resources expended on the various 
communication channels?
What feedback did the City receive on the project/program/policy during the roll-
out?
•	 Was this feedback incorporated already when mitigations were made?
•	 Are there changes that should be made going forward to improve the outcome?
•	 Are you tracking the feedback by population (or by other demographic indicators)? 
10
354 of 1058

--- page 355 ---
11
Looking to the Future
The DEI lens created within this strategic framework is designed to be applied to the 
creation and development of all future programs, policies, and projects in order to 
anticipate impact and mitigate the potential for furthering inequality. 
The value of the framework is its provision of clarification before implementation. It 
addresses the ultimate questions of; what are we doing, why are we doing it, and will 
our plans result in the intended outcomes? The City plans to integrate this framework 
into our daily operations and make it a seamless part of how we serve our community.
In addition, the critical questions in this strategic framework offer structure for the 
analysis of the impacts of our current programs, projects, and policies. As a second 
phase of this strategic planning process, the City will perform a comprehensive audit 
of our current practices using this framework. The City will use the outcome of that 
audit to make adjustments to our existing approach to ensure that our services 
reflect our commitment to the DEI principles outlined in this Strategic Plan.

FROM:

PREPARED BY:
Community Development Department
MEETING DATE:
May 15, 2023 
SUBJECT:
100 N Humboldt St. – Property Owner Appeal of Permanent Relocation 
RECOMMENDATION:
Deny the appeal and uphold the notice to reimburse the City of San Mateo for the permanent relocation benefits to the 
tenants who reside at 100 N. Humboldt St. due to unlawful substandard living conditions pursuant to San Mateo Municipal 
Code Chapter 7.50 “Property Owner Obligations with Respect to Tenants Displaced from Unsafe or Substandard Units.” 
BACKGROUND:
History
On January 10, 2023 the City received a report from the tenants of 100 N. Humboldt St. (the “Property”) stating that:
•
None of the home’s four heaters were in working order
•
The tenant had informed the landlord the roof was leaking over a year ago and the landlord had put a tarp over it 
instead of repairing it
•
A portion of the ceiling had collapsed in the kitchen the night before, nearly injuring a tenant
•
A different portion of the ceiling had previously collapsed in hallway
•
Rats had been entering the home through holes in the roof/ceiling, and the tenant’s husband had patched them 
since the landlord would not make repairs
•
The kitchen sink and shower lacked hot water and though the landlord replaced the water heater, there was still no 
hot water
The tenants also reported they had been served an eviction notice on November 17, 2022 and had requested an extension 
since they had not found a new residence, but the request had been denied. On Friday, January 13, 2023 City staff inspected 
the residence and confirmed the following conditions (see Attachment 1 – January 13, 2023 Inspection Photos):
1.
The roof had multiple leaks and was covered with a tarp
2.
Visible mold on the walls and ceilings of the bathroom and two bedrooms
3.
Lack of hot water reaching 110 degrees
4.
Lack of heating
5.
Multiple electrical hazards (daisy-chained cords and missing outlet covers, light fixtures, and switches)
6.
No smoke detectors
7.
Termite damage
8.
Improper tiling/waterproofing of shower/bathtub
9.
Deteriorated front door framing
10. Missing front door weather stripping
11. Inoperable sliding glass door
12. Deteriorated windows throughout residence
13. Unpermitted wall removal
358 of 1058

--- page 359 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
14. Inadequate water flow in kitchen
15. Improper/leaky plumbing
16. Dilapidated kitchen cabinetry caused by water damage
17. Deteriorated walls with holes throughout residence
18. Missing gas stove exhaust vent
19. Smell of gas and inoperable oven
20. New windows, improperly installed
21. Stair risers not to Code
22. Exterior holes caused by unpermitted electrical work
Between January 17 and 26, 2023, City staff met internally to research the violations and confer with the tenant to obtain 
information about how the violations came to be, when they informed the landlord of Property conditions, and any work 
they/the landlord performed on the residence. During this time it was concluded, pursuant to San Mateo Municipal Code 
(“SMMC “) section 7.14, the International Property Maintenance Code (“IPMC”) and California Health and Safety Code 
section 17920.3, the Property was unsafe to inhabit, the landlord appeared to have had knowledge of the primary Property 
conditions which made it unsafe to occupy (i.e. the failed roof, extensive mold, and lack of heat and hot water) and had not 
corrected them. Thus, staff proceeded with issuing a Notice to Vacate at the owner’s expense on January 26, 2023 
(Attachment 2—January 26, 2013 Inspection Photos) and requiring vacation to be completed by January 30, 2023.  Pursuant 
to SMMC section 7.50.020(i), the vacation was determined to be permanent since the work needed to restore the Property 
would likely exceed 90 days.  On January 26, 2023 the property owner, Ms. Trina Pierce (“Property Owner”), responded by 
telephone, requesting the City revise the relocation benefit to reflect the number of bedrooms on record, rather than the 
number in use, and informed staff she had replaced the water heater. She was informed that there was no permit issued for 
the water heater, there was still no hot water being delivered, and the City would consider revising the amount of the 
relocation benefit to reflect the HUD rate for a three-bedroom unit per her request.
On January 27, 2023, Ms. Pierce and her attorney, Ms. Virginia Papan, visited City Hall and informed staff they would be 
providing documentation by January 30, 2023 disputing the relocation expense alleging the Property conditions were caused 
by the tenants. Between January 27 and 30, 2023, staff had several communications with Ms. Pierce and Ms. Papan and 
continued to make adjustments to how the case was being handled to ensure the tenants were safe, the structure was 
secured, and the landlord was able to weigh in with her version of the situation.  
On January 30, 2023, the City conducted another inspection, which included the Building Inspection Supervisor, in addition 
to Code Enforcement staff (Attachment 3 -January 30, 2023 Inspection Photos). During the inspection, the condition of the 
roof was deemed to be in such poor condition as to be an immediate threat to life-safety, there was still no hot water, and 
pursuant to SMMC Chapter 7.14, the IPMC, the California Health and Safety Code (17920.3), and the Dangerous Building 
Code (SMMC Chapter 23.30), the structure was Red Tagged (Attachment 4 – Red Tag).  The Property Owner did not appeal 
the Red-Tag determination. On January 31, 2023, the property owner submitted several documents (Attachment 5 – 
Documents from Property Owner) intended to support her position that she unaware of the life-safety violations associated 
with the residence. Based on the City’s review, on February 1, 2023 an amended vacation notice (Attachment 6 – Amended 
Notice to Vacate) was sent to the Property Owner. The amendments reduced the relocation payment amount to reflect a 3-
bedroom residence, per the legal record, and reduced the number of violations being cited as the responsibility of the 
Property Owner. On February 3, 2023, pursuant to SMMC section 7.50.070, the City issued the relocation payment of 
$12,736 to the tenant and the tenants vacated the residence (Attachment 7—Relocation Check to Tenant). The Property 
Owner then submitted a permit application for a new roof on February 7, 2023 (Attachment 8—Reroof Permit Application), 
and on February 9, 2023 submitted an appeal of the relocation costs (Attachment 9—Property Owner Appeal).
Appeal
Pursuant to SMMC section 7.50.030(a), a tenant household is eligible for relocation payments from a property owner if the 
tenant household is displaced from its dwelling due to the City’s code enforcement activities, including when the City has 
issued a notice to vacate, notice to abate a life-threatening condition, or declaration of substandard condition covering the 
dwelling.  A property owner is not required to make relocation payments if the property owner demonstrates to the 
satisfaction of the Community Development Director that the uninhabitable conditions are caused by the tenant household, 
359 of 1058

--- page 360 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
the tenant household unreasonably prevented the property owner from maintenance that would have prevented the 
condition, or the conditions are solely from damage from an earthquake, fire, flood, natural disaster, civil disturbance, or 
accident outside the control of the property owner and was not caused by or acts or negligence by the Property or due to 
pre-existing conditions or violations. (SMMC §§ 7.50.030(b)(2), (b3), (b)(7).)  Pursuant to the appeal, the Property Owner 
claims she should not have to pay relocation costs for the identified ten reasons described and analyzed below:
1.
Tenants caused mold at the premises by failing to keep it clean and concealed these conditions from the 
owner—This reason claims the tenants caused mold by not keeping the residence clean, putting up and taking 
down walls, altering cabinetry, punching holes in walls and doors, eating and making food in bedrooms, keeping 
windows/doors shut and covered, not notifying her of mold conditions, intimidating her, and limiting her access 
to certain parts of the residence.  
Response: To staff’s knowledge and experience, dirt, eating or making food in bedrooms, and the type of holes that result 
from punching a wall or door, would not typically be a contributing factor to the formation of excessive mold conditions. 
The type of mold under consideration is not food mold. While the alteration of walls or cabinetry that allows water entry 
into a building and keeping a structure fully enclosed in excessively moist conditions may contribute to mold formation, the 
Property Owner has not provided supporting documentation to substantiate this assertion. Though the City ordered the 
Property Owner to provide a mold report in January, which would have provided information about the source and extent 
of the mold found in the bedrooms and bathroom, as well as methods for its removal, the report (Attachment 10—Mold 
Report) was not prepared until after the roof was replaced in late February 2023. It was then revised in March 2023 and 
finally submitted to the City in late April 2023, after several requests from the City. The report does not address the source 
of the mold; however, it clearly identifies substantial abatement is necessary to correct the condition.
Staff is unaware if the tenants reported mold to the landlord but is aware they reported the defective roof and leaking 
ceilings in need of repair, allowing moisture into the residence. Specifically, the tenants submitted text messages between 
themselves and the landlord from October 2022 (Attachment 11—Text Messages) demonstrating they had reported the 
defective roof and leak in the kitchen in October 2022 and the landlord covered the roof with a tarp instead of making 
immediate repairs. Furthermore, the Property Owner received at least one written quote for a roof repair, dated October 
26, 2022, and chose not to perform the repair (Attachment 12—Roof Estimate). The kitchen ceiling eventually collapsed 
near one of the tenants’ head on January 9, 2023. The tenants also informed staff verbally on January 18 and 26, 2023 that 
they had informed the landlord of the roof defects and other interior leaks and plumbing defects as early as 2020, and that 
Ms. Pierce had also covered the roof with a tarp at that time. The text messages support this and imply the tenants paid for 
some type of roof/ceiling repairs. Staff also reviewed Google street view images of this corner lot dating back to 2018 
(Attachment 13—Google Images) and the roof defects are visible from the Humboldt and Tilton street frontages in multiple 
images. In addition, the Property Owner’s own photographs, prior to the tenants’ occupation, show a deteriorating roof 
(Attachment 4, Exhibit D.)  The defects/leaks with which staff is most familiar are along the structure perimeter or along roof 
joints, which seem to be unlikely locations for interior partition walls or cabinet doors. It is noteworthy that the Property 
Owner lives close by and can, and does, easily view the Property on a regular basis. This is confirmed, as she readily 
approached staff each time staff visited the Property. Another possible source of moisture entry is the exterior wall openings 
where repairs were not made following a permitted electrical service upgrade.
As far as the tenants limiting Ms. Pierce’s access, that is matter between her and her tenants. In item 7 of this appeal, the 
Property Owner acknowledges she accessed the bedrooms, in which a large amount of the mold was located. If this is true, 
she would have had visual access to mold on the walls and ceiling of the bedrooms, as it was extensive and easily visible. If 
she felt intimidated or unsafe, as stated in the appeal, and that is why her access was limited, she had the option of enlisting 
police or legal assistance at any time. Again, that is a matter between the Property Owner and tenants, to which the City is 
not a party.
2.
There were no hot water issues, nor did the Tenants ever inform the Owner of such an issue—This reason 
states that in May 2022 the tenants reported the water heater needed repair and the landlord replaced it and 
the tenants did not report any need for further repairs. It also refers to the lease, to which the City is not a 
party.
360 of 1058

--- page 361 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
Response: In May 2022, the Property Owner replaced the water heater without the required permit, so it cannot be verified 
whether it was done correctly. The tenants reported to City staff that the new water heater never worked correctly.  During 
its inspection on January 13, 2023, the City confirmed a lack of hot water reaching bathroom and kitchen fixtures. When the 
Property Owner received the City’s notice on January 26, 2023, she replaced the water heater without a permit and the new 
water heater failed to deliver hot water to the residence. The permit was finally obtained, and the work corrected on March 
16, 2023 (Attachment 14—Water Heater Permit). It is unclear whether the tenants notified the landlord of the continued 
lack of hot water after the May 2022 replacement. However, obtaining the required permit to ensure the work was 
performed correctly would have provided some assurance the repair would be both legal and successful. 
3.
Tenants never notified the owner about lack of heat—This reason states the tenants had full possession of 
the residence and never notified the owner about the lack of heat.
Response: The tenants informed staff on multiple occasions that none of the four heaters within residence were functional 
when they leased the house, and that the landlord was aware of this condition from the onset.  Rather, the tenants stated 
they were required to rely on space heaters throughout the residence.  During its inspections, staff verified the heaters did 
not function and space heaters are depicted in staff’s inspection photographs.
4.
Tenants created and maintained electrical hazards—This reason claims the tenants installed new plugs and 
outlets and made other changes inside the residence, which they then concealed from her, after she upgraded 
the electrical service.
Response: These conditions were inside the residence, were generalized, but in small spaces. Staff believes it is reasonable 
to believe the landlord may not have been aware of the extent of these conditions.  
5.
The landlord is not liable for water leaking in—This reason is reference to roof leaks and cites extreme weather 
events of December 2022 and January 2023, and walls erected by the tenants the causes of the roof defects 
and leaks. It also states the tenant failed to notify the landlord of these conditions until January 2023. 
Response: See response to item 1 above, which explains that the Property Owner was notified well before December 2022 
of the roof and ceiling defects, that the defects were visible from two street frontages, and that it is not substantiated that 
the several locations of the defects correlate with any alterations made by the tenants. 
6.
The tenants removed the workings smoke detectors.
Response: The text message correspondence implies this may be the case. 
7.
The tenant’s failure to clean and maintain the premises caused the infestation of rodents and pests—This 
reason claims the tenants took occupancy of the house without rodents/pests and therefore caused the 
infestation by living in squalor, eating in bedrooms and leaving half eaten food in them.
Response: First, it is of note that this assertion admits the landlord had knowledge of the conditions of the bedrooms, but 
denies having had knowledge of the extensive mold that was within them and claims the tenants blocked her from accessing 
many parts of the residence. Nonetheless, while eating and having food in the bedrooms may have exacerbated rodent/pest 
conditions, holes in roofs and exterior walls are known entry points for rats and insects. This house had several entry points 
which provided easy and continued access to infestations. The record shows the Property Owner was aware of the roof 
conditions and did not make repairs. The record concerning the electrical service upgrade shows the project took an 
excessive duration and was overly complicated. The finished product shows that subsequent exterior wall repairs were not 
completed. The window replacements, some done with permits, left behind unrepaired termite damage. The implication is 
that there is a history of the Property Owner contracting for subpar work that does not get completed properly or 
completely, if at all, and that has left the structure vulnerable to a variety of problems, such as rodent/pest infestations, in 
addition to the mold described in item 1.  
361 of 1058

--- page 362 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
8.
Owner had no knowledge the home had termite damage
Response: Termite damage should have been observed and reported on by the professionals Ms. Pierce hired for window 
installations, as this would be known to them and not be something easily identifiable by the tenants. The tenants did report 
to Ms. Pierce via text and a photograph of the damaged area that there were problems with the window installations and 
asked her to look them.
9.
The tenants restricted lighting and ventilation
Response: It is unclear to staff whether the tenants altered/relocated light fixtures. The provided correspondence does 
support that the tenants erected and dismantled partition walls in the living and dining rooms.
10. The tenants never notified the owner about any possible gas leak
Response:  Though staff readily smelled gas when the residence was inspected, it is unknown when this condition began or 
whether the tenant reported it to the landlord. 
Public Comment
The City received one public comment in support of Ms. Pierce (Attachment 15—Public Comment). The comment appears 
to be from a previous tenant of the subject property, who reports that Ms. Pierce was responsive to repair issues and also 
that she provided/allowed food facilities in the bedrooms and that the pilot light upstairs was turned off for the heating.
Conclusion
Following the tenants’ vacation, the landlord procured a permit to begin work on the new roof, and eventually pulled a 
permit to legalize and modify the unpermitted water heater. After several requests from staff, the landlord also eventually 
provided the mold report on April 21, 2023 and submitted to an inspection of the residence interior by City staff on May 5, 
2023 after a Final Notice form the City (Attachments 16 and 17). The inspection revealed extensive demolition work had 
been completed without the necessary permits, despite the fact the landlord had been advised several times of the need to 
obtain permits for demolition/mold remediation work. This includes City permits and permits from the Bay Area Air Quality 
Control District. The permits are needed to ensure not only the safety of the workers on-site, but also to protect those in 
the project vicinity from hazardous materials such as lead, asbestos, and the mold itself. This is especially true of older 
structures such as this one, which was built in 1940. Staff believes this provides further evidence of the landlord’s disregard 
for public safety and her pattern of misconduct. It also further demonstrates her disregard for the safety codes that govern 
her property. Accordingly, and as demonstrated above, Ms. Pierce was knowledgeable of and is responsible for the most 
egregious property conditions that rendered the property uninhabitable. Specifically, the mold, roof and ceiling defects, and 
rodent and pest infestations.  Of note, the roof and ceiling defects may be considered the most critical of the uninhabitable 
conditions as it may be a cause for other uninhabitable conditions such as water damage and mold issues. Staff also believes 
the Property Owner is responsible for and had, or should have had, knowledge of the termite damage, leaks, and defective 
water heater and heaters based on a review of communications between her and the tenant household. Accordingly, staff 
recommends that the City Council deny the appeal and order the landlord to reimburse the City for the tenant relocation 
payment of $12,736. 
BUDGET IMPACT:
There is no budget impact related to this report. If Council finds that City funds should be used to provide relocation benefits 
to the tenant, the money would be allocated from the Emergency Relocation Assistance Fund that was established to 
advance tenant assistance in urgent situations and then billed to the property owner. 
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
This appeal is not a project subject to CEQA, because it is an organizational or administrative activity that will not result in 
direct or indirect physical changes in the environment. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(5).)

CITY OF SAN MATEO
COMMUNITY DEVELOPMENT DEPARTMENT

01/26/2023
WARNING LETTER TO ABATE A PUBLIC NUISANCE
AND DECLARATION OF SUBSTANDARD DWELLING CONDITION AND NOTICE TO VACATE

Trina M Pierce Tr CASE: CE-2023-000017
APN: 033122160
Address: 100 N Humboldt St

You are receiving this letter as the record owner of the property located at 100 N Humboldt St in San
Mateo, California. The City of San Mateo Code Enforcement received a complaint regarding
violations of the San Mateo Municipal Code (SMMC) existing on your property. Based on that
information, Code Enforcement Staff conducted an inspection of the property on 01/13/2023 and
confirmed multiple life safety and substandard conditions. This isa formal notice from the City of San
Mateo to correct the violations.

Immediate Life Safety & Substandard Conditions:

1. Visible mold in both the walls and ceilings located in the bathroom and in two bedrooms,

2. Lack of hot water reaching the minimum temperature of 110 degrees,

3. Lack of required heat in the dwelling,

4. Multiple electrical hazards throughout the house, (including, daisy chained extension cords,
broken and missing outlet covers, missing light fixtures, and switches),

5. Multiple ceiling and roof holes causing water leaks and damage throughout the dwelling,

6. Lack of required smoke detectors in entire dwelling,

7. Rodents/pests in the dwelling,

8. Termite damage.

Other Violations:
1. The bathroom shower and bathtub are not properly tiled, sealed, or waterproofed to prevent
water intrusion,
Deteriorated front door and framing,
Missing weather stripping in the front door,
Nonoperating sliding glass door,
Deteriorated windows throughout the dwelling,
Removal of an interior wall in the living room,
Low hot water flow in the kitchen,
Improper plumbing alterations that are causing leaks,
Dilapidated cabinets due to leaking/improper plumbing in the kitchen sink,
10, Missing kitchen exhaust vent for gas burning stove,
11. Deteriorated interior walls and holes throughout the dwelling,
12. Installation of new windows,

WOON DNARWHN

Housing Division (650) 522-7220 - Planning Division (650) 522-7212 - General (650) 522-7200

VIRGINIA I. PAPAN

January 30, 2023

Ms. Kristen Aiu

330 W. 20" Avenue

Re: Case No. CE-2023-000017 at 100 N. Humboldt Street

Dear Ms. Aiu,

I write in response to the City of San Mateo’s Abatement Notice (City’s Notice) and timeline,
which you placed on hold on F riday, January 27, 2023. Thank you for the opportunity to provide
you with the context, background, and documentation concerning Trina Pierce’s (Landlord)
property at 100 N. Humboldt Street, San Mateo, California (the "Premises").

This is a case of tenants gaming the system to extract relocation costs and exploit a senior in the
process. It is particularly tragic given that they destroyed the treasured family home of the
Landlord's deceased mother. The Tenants have been systematically violating the lease. When
they received the Landlord's “Notice to Vacate” they went into overdrive, damaging the property
and then calling in code enforcement to use the City and its laws as a sword to extract money
from the Landlord. There is no basis for the claims against the Landlord, the Tenants destroyed
the Premises. They should vacate and the Landlord should not have to pay money to them for
destroying her property.

I. LANDLORD’S NOVEMBER 17, 2022 NOTICE TO VACATE AND TENANTS' DESIRE TO
STAY

The Premises are the treasured home that Trina Pierce/Landlord inherited from her mother. In
2017, Landlord leased the Premises to Herminia Panameno, Josue N. Panameno, Patricia
Panameno, and Marta Zavala (Tenants). (Exhibits A-C hereto are the written lease and two
amended agreements, hereinafter collectively referred to as the “Lease”.) When Landlord turned
Over possession of her family’s home to Tenants, it was clean, sanitary and in good repair.
(Exhibit D shows photos of the condition of the Premises when Tenants took possession.)
Thereafter until the present, Tenants maintained the exclusive possession and control of the

552 of 1058


--- page 553 ---
Kristen Aiu, Code Enforcement Officer II

Re: Case #CE-2023-000017 — 100 N. Humboldt St., San Mateo
Page 2 of 3

Premises. Landlord believed when the tenancy ended the home would be returned in the same
condition as existed when Tenants moved in, except for normal wear and tear.

On November 17, 2022, Landlord served Tenants with a 60-day Notice to Vacate (Exhibit E
hereto) for the following breaches of the Lease:

1. No Pets — Tenants have 4 dogs and chicken on the Premises

2. Damages caused by occupants entering the Premises through a window which
Landlord told Tenants multiple times not to do, and caused the Landlord to replace the
window three times.

3. Tenants secretly operating a business at the Premises, advertised by Mendez, someone
the Landlord does not know.

4. Tenants altering the Premises - More and more walls going up, without permits or
permission from the Landlord;

5. Continuing to have people moving in and out of the premises without Landlord’s
knowledge or permission;

6. Fire risks, continually placing things in front of a heater, which Landlord told Tenants
not to do.

7. Overflowing garbage and trash bins, uncovered, and bags of trash by the bins — a health
issue which can cause rodent problems. Trash and beer cans visible from front yard
needed to be moved to back yard.

Most importantly, after receiving the Notice to Vacate Tenants still tried to pay Landlord rent to
continue to live at the habitable Premises and requested that they be allowed to stay. Landlord
refused to accept the rent or allow Tenants to stay longer. She continues to want Tenants to
vacate because of their repeated breaches of the Lease. She advised them that she was upset that
they continued to not care or even try to address the issues of people climbing out windows,
causing Landlord to replace the window three times, 4 dogs, and the need to do a lot of work.
Tenants responded by admitting to breaking some rules like subleasing and explaining it does
not stay clean because of other people. !

-_

* Exhibit F shows recent text messages from Tenants admitting they were violating the lease by subleasing, breaking
some rules, and explaining it doesn’t stay clean because of other people.

553 of 1058


--- page 554 ---
Kristen Aiu, Code Enforcement Officer II
Re: Case #CE-2023-000017 — 100 N. Humboldt St., San Mateo
Page 2 of 3

Thereafter, Landlord learned that her Notice to Vacate was insufficient as it did not expressly
state the names of each tenant.? While in the process of re-serving the notice to vacate, Landlord
was shocked to receive the City’s Notice advising that the Premises were uninhabitable, Tenants
must relocate and that Landlord must pay Tenants close to $14,000 within 5 days. While
Landlord wants Tenants out of her treasured home, she objects to having to pay displacement,
relocation, and moving fees as Tenants destroyed the Premises.

Il. TENANTS Dip Not PROVIDE LANDLORD NOTICE OF DEFICIENCIES OR AN
OPPORTUNITY TO CURE.

Tenants failed to notify Landlord and allow Landlord an opportunity to cure the alleged
substandard conditions in the City’s Notice. The Lease mandates that Tenants immediately
notify Landlord of needed repairs and provide Landlord an opportunity to cure the conditions.
In fact, Tenants had always followed this notice procedure throughout their tenancy and Landlord
provided the needed repairs. (Exhibit G: Landlord’s Repairs) It is only now after Tenants
received a Notice to Vacate that Tenants are claiming uninhabitable conditions to the City,
conditions which as explained below they created and concealed from Landlord.

The Lease expressly provides that Tenants shall “immediately notify Landlord of any defects or
dangerous conditions in and about the Premises of which they become aware” and if Tenants or
their guests cause the damages they shall “reimburse Landlord, ... for the cost of any repairs to
the premises, ... damaged by Tenants or their guest or invitees through misuse or neglect.” (See
Exs. A-C, Rental Agreements, paragraph 11 sections (2) and (3).)

Throughout the tenancy and prior to the Landlord’s Notice to Vacate, Tenants would notify
Landlord, in writing via text messages, of any needed repairs to the Premises and Landlord would
make the repairs. Landlord has extensive records documenting repairs made and replacing
fixtures, including windows, a water heater, and electrical panel. (Ex. G: Landlord’s Repairs)

On January 1, 2023, following extraordinary rains from multiple atmospheric rivers, Tenants
notified Landlord that the roof was leaking and water had entered the Premises. Landlord
immediately took actions to mitigate water coming in and began contracting with a roofer for a
permanent repair. (Exhibit H: Landlord’s Contract for Roof Replacement.) Tenants also made
a vague reference to a plumbing issue on J. anuary 1. Landlord retained a plumber to inspect and
repair the item. It will be repaired within the week of J anuary 30th.

Aside from the recent leaking roof and plumbing issue, Tenants did not notify Landlord about
other damages or needed repairs as identified in the City’s Notice, nor did they provide Landlord

* Landlord’s Notice to Vacate gave Tenants 60 days to vacate. Had Landlord known she could have served a three-
day notice to vacate for breach of the Lease she would have done so. See California Code of Civil Procedure, section
1161(4), which provides in relevant part as follows: any tenant, assigning or subletting or committing waste upon the
premises, contrary to the conditions of the lease, or maintaining, committing, or permitting the maintenance or
commission of a nuisance upon the premises or using the premises for an unlawful purpose thereby terminates the
lease, and the Landlord is entitled to restitution of possession of the premises.

with an opportunity to cure. Moreover, as set forth below Tenants had exclusive possession and

Il]. TENANTS COMMITTED WASTE ON THE PROPERTY AND SHOULD Not BENEFIT
FROM THEIR DESTRUCTION OF LANDLORD’S FAMILY HOME

This is not a case of a landlord renting a substandard unit. Quite the contrary, Landlord rented
her family home in a habitable condition. (See Exhibit D photos). Tenants who had exclusive
control over the Premises repeatedly breached the Lease causing waste and creating dangerous
conditions. After receiving a Notice to Vacate, Tenants caused further damages to the Property
and then complained to Code Enforcement in an effort to exploit the system. They should not
be able to profit from their misconduct and destruction of Landlord’s family home.

Tenants signed the February 2017 rental agreement and amended agreements in June 2018 and
January 2019, agreeing that the Premises at 100 N. Humboldt Street was clean, sanitary, in good
repair, free of infestations of rodents, cockroaches and other insects and pests, and included
working smoke detectors. (See Exhibits A-C, paragraphs 11 and 20 and Exhibit D photos.)

Since taking possession of the Premise Tenants repeatedly breached the Lease by committing
waste, altering the structure, failing to clean and maintain the Premises causing a nuisance,
deterioration, and infestations. Exhibit I hereto contains photos of the damages caused by shows
Tenants misconduct and destruction of the Premise including the following:

e Putting up and taking down walls to subdivide the structure for subtenants and removing
them leaving holes in the ceiling and walls, ruining the mirror, fireplace, and floors
Damaging windows through misuse — Photos of people using window for entry and exit
Destroying cabinets and countertops installed only two years earlier in the Kitchen
Removed light fixtures & Window coverings

Extreme wear and scratches on the floors

Removed carpets from back room joining kitchen

Holes in the walls, cabinets, and doors

Removed Smoke detectors

Allowing unauthorized residents to live on the Premises

Keeping pets, chickens and other fowl without permission or permits

* The City’s Letter seems to claim that Landlord should have inspected the Premises. There is no basis for this claim.
A Landlord is not required to conduct an inspection nor did this Landlord have any reason to believe that any
inspection was needed as the Tenants would always close off all the doors whenever they allowed Landlord into
the Premises for repairs and intimidated her into staying within the area where the repairs were being made.
As such, Landlord was not able to view the conditions throughout the Premises that would indicate a further inspection
was needed.

Tenants and for no other purpose without Landlord’s prior written consent. Occupancy

Defining the Term of the Tenancy: The rental shall begin on

__ 3/1/2017 .and shall continue on a month-to-month basis. This
Tenancy may be terminated by Landlord or Tenants and may be modified by Landlord,
by giving 30 days’ written notice to the other, or 60 days notice by Landlord to Tena nt,
in accordance with the Civil Code, Section 827 or 1946.1, subject to any local rent

Amount and Schedule for the Payment of Rent: Tenants shall pay to Landlord a
monthly rent is $3500.00, tenant and Landlord agree the monthly rent will be reduced
to $3400.00. Rent will be deducted by $100.00 per month due to Tenant(s) has agreed
to maintain yard at 100 N. Humboldt and back yard at home located at 1016 Tilton Ave,
San Mateo, 94401 payable in advance or before the first day of each calendar month,
except when that day falls on a weekend or legal holiday, in which case rent is due on
the next business day. Rent shall be paid to Trina Pierce or another person if Landlord
so designates from time to time. Form of payment shall be personal check, certified
funds or money order._No Cash will be accepted.

Late Charges: Payments received later than 5:00pm on the fifth day of any month shall
incur a late charge of $50.00. ,

Returned Check and Other Bank Charges: In the event any check offered by Tenants to
Landlord in payment of rent or any other amount due under this Agreement is returned
for lack of sufficient funds, a “stop Payment” or any other reason, Tenants shall pay
Landlord a returned check charge in the amount of $35.00. Should check be returned,
Landiord has the right to require that future payments be made by money order,
cashier’s check or certified check.

three weeks after Tenants have vacated the premises, Landlord shall furnish Tenants

took occupancy, except for ordinary wear and tear; (2) immediately notify Landlord
of any defects or dangerous conditions in and about the premises of which they
become aware; (3) reimburse Landlord, on demand by Landlord; for the cost of any

Tenants or their guest or invitees through misuse or neglect. The premises are being
delivered to tenant free of infestations of ants, rodents, fleas, cockroaches, and other

follow up with a written notice. Landlord agrees to pay for extermination cost, but
should the infestation continue after being exterminated, then the responsibiiity will

November 17, 2022

To: All the Tenants Residing at 100 N. Humboldt St
This notice serves as your 60 day notice to vacate the property at 100 N. Humboldt St.

The notice is pertaining to all the people listed on the Lease Agreement and all those living in
the home, who are not listed on the Lease Agreement.

Attached with this notice is a copy of the AB1482, which covers legal protection of both the
Landlord and Renter’s rights. You are being evicted for a breach of material terms of the Lease.
You will see by law you are not protected by the Tenant Protection Act (AB1482).

Due to the damage that has been caused to the property, all individuals must vacate by
January 17, 2023. Due to the various damages caused to the interior and exterior of the
property it will require major repairs and renovations.

Because of the many Lease Violation extreme da mage has been thrust_upon the property at
100 N. Humboldt St

Violations:

1) Lease Agreement states NO Pets. Yet, you have 4 dogs living on the property. Also,
Chickens, now there are 4 baby chicks. No permission was given or even asked.

2) Multiple times you have been told not to enter and exit thru the window. This action has
caused damage and | have had to replace the window 3 times. Yet you still continue to
disregard my request.

3) The property is for residential use only. Yet, you have allowed an advertising by Mendez to
use the address of 100 N. Humboldt St, San Mateo as your business. No one asked could a
business be run out of my rental home. Which would be a violation and a fine charged to

me. | do not even know this person This Violation Must stop Immediatedly. The post will
need to be removed from Next Door and any other Advertisement.

4) More and more walls are going up altering the home, which continues without my
permission. This again is a violation and | can be fined.

5) You continue to have people move into the residence without my knowledge or my
permission

6) Continually you have been asked not place anything in front of the heater. Whether the
heater is on or off, due to a fire risk and a fire violation.

NOTICE LANGUAGE REQUIREMENTS IN THE TENANT PROTECTION ACT OF 2019
(AB 1482)

1. REQUIRED NOTICE LANGUAGE for tenancies in units that are EXEMPT from the Tenant
Protection Act because the property meets the requirements detailed in Civil Code §§
1947.12(d)(5) and 1946.2(e)(8). Specifically, this would be a “residential real property that
is alienable separate from the title to any other dwelling unit” with no corporate
ownership. E.g. single-family home on its own parcel, condominium.

The paragraph below should be included in any lease entered into after July 1, 2020. For
existing tenancies, a notice with this paragraph should be provided to the tenant.

“This property is not subject to the rent limits imposed by Section 1947.12 of the Civil
Code and is not subject to the just cause requirements of Section 1946.2 of the Civil
Code. This property meets the requirements of Sections 1947.12 (d)(5) and 1946.2
(e)(8) of the Civil Code and the owner is not any of the following: (1) a real estate
investment trust, as defined by Section 856 of the Internal Revenue Code; (2) a
corporation; or (3) a limited liability company in which at least one member is a
corporation.”

2. REQUIRED NOTICE LANGUAGE for tenancies in units that are NOT EXEMPT from the
Tenant Protection Act.

- For tenancies existing prior to July 1, 2020, a notice with the language below must be
given to the tenant no later than August 1, 2020, or as an addendum to the lease or
rental agreement.

- For tenancies beginning or being renewed on or after July 1, 2020, language below
must be included as an addendum to the lease or rental agreement, or as a written
notice signed by the tenant, with a copy provided to the tenant.

- The notice or lease provision shall be in no less than 12-point type, in the language
the original contract was negotiated in, and shall include the following:

“California law limits the amount your rent can be increased. See Section 1947.12 of
the Civil Code for more information. California law also provides that after all of the
tenants have continuously and lawfully occupied the property for 12 months or more
or at least one of the tenants has continuously and lawfully occupied the property for
24 months or more, a landlord must provide a statement of cause in any notice to
terminate a tenancy. See Section 1946.2 of the Civil Code for more information.”

*This flyer is for informational use only*

for your tenant's comfort. | found no apparent electrical problem within the circuit wiring itself
, but likely it may be a problem with too many people, plugging in too much for the circuit to
handle, ( which could mean more appliances connecting to an electrical " branch circuit" with
not enough "power supply" for the equipment connected.)

A AWED Electric determined there were appliances, extension cords used for refrigerators,
heaters plugged in, old devices, as well as previous construction work which did not appear
up to code! The existing electrical panel work appeared either to have been tampered with
because the work was shoddy, potentially dangerous, as well as illegal; strict code violations
such as electrical metallic tubing entering metal enclosures without connectors which
eliminates a necessary mechanical safety bond. Sub-Panel behind the kitchen wall is two feet
off of the ground( A height violation).

Panels have no ground continuity! Splicing within the existing sub-panel (by previous
installer)due to lack of electrical circuits within the panel, which tends to cause overloads
(power outages).
